No Amputation for Stealing a Cross, Chess, or Backgammon
Ruling that hand is not cut for stealing a cross, chess set, or backgammon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that amputation is not applied for stealing a cross, chess, or backgammon, explain the concept of mal mutaqawwim, and cite the Quranic and hadith proofs.

Core Idea
A stolen item must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for amputation; symbols of disbelief and forbidden playthings are not.

In Hanafi hudud law, the punishment of hand amputation for theft is not applied arbitrarily; it depends on strict conditions, one of which is that the stolen item must be considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). This lesson examines three specific items – a cross (even of gold), a chess set, and a backgammon set – whose theft does not trigger the hadd. The reasoning is rooted in their religious and legal status: these items are either symbols of disbelief or instruments of frivolous play, and therefore are not recognized as lawful property in Islamic law. We will explore the proofs from the Quran and Sunnah that support this ruling and understand the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
List the three items and their apparent value
Identify the three items mentioned: a cross of gold/silver, a chess set, and a backgammon set. Note that each has material value but is treated differently in Hanafi law.
The cross may be made of precious metal, yet it is not considered mal mutaqawwim.
2
Define 'mal mutaqawwim' in Hanafi law
Recall the condition for amputation: the stolen item must be mal mutaqawwim – property that is legally recognized as valuable and permissible to own and benefit from. Items that are inherently unlawful or have no lawful benefit are not mal mutaqawwim.
This is a key distinction: material value alone is insufficient; legal recognition is required.
3
Apply the condition to each item
Explain why each item fails the mal mutaqawwim test: the cross is a symbol of disbelief with no lawful benefit, chess and backgammon are frivolous playthings that are forbidden to play, and therefore have negligible or no legal value.
Even if the cross is gold, its form and purpose render it non-mutaqawwim.
4
Examine the Quranic proof
Read the verse (Luqman 31:6) which condemns 'idle talk' (lahw al-hadith). The Hanafi interpretation includes games like chess and backgammon. Since the Quran censures them, they cannot be considered valuable property.
The verse is qati' in transmission but zanni in application, yet it sufficiently supports the ruling.
5
Examine the hadith proofs
Review the hadith on backgammon (dipping hand in pig flesh) and the hadith on the kubah (drum) from Abu Dawud, plus the athar from Ibn Abbas. These show that the items are haram, and thus not suitable for legal ownership or theft valuation.
These proofs collectively establish that the items lack lawful benefit, the crux of non-mutaqawwim status.
6
Note the cross-madhhab nuance
Consider that Imam al-Shafi'i may view the material value of a gold cross as sufficient for amputation. Contrast this with the Hanafi position that the form (cross) overrides the material value.
This ikhtilaf illustrates how different usul principles lead to different rulings.

Source Text

ولا في الصليب من الذهب والفضة ولا الشطرنج ولا النرد. صلیب نصاری کے پوجنے کے لئے ہے جو نا جائز ہے اور غیر متقوم ہے۔ اور شطرنج اور نر کھیل کودکی چیز ہے جو نئیں نہیں بلکہ حقیر ہے۔ اس لئے ان کے چرانے میں بھی ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) اس کی حرمت کی دلیل آیت میں ہے۔ ومن الناس من يشترى لهو الحديث ليضل عن سبيل الله بغير علم ويتخذها هزوا اولئك لهم عذاب مهین (ب) ( آیت ۶ سورۃ لقمان (۳۱) اس آیت میں کھیل کود کی چیزوں کی برائی بیان کی ہے۔ اور نردشیر کے بارے میں یہ حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن سلیمان بن بريدة عن ابيه ان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال من لعب بالنرد شير فكأنما صبغ يده فى لحم خنزیر و دمه (ج) (مسلم شریف، باب تحریم اللعب بالنردشیر ص ۲۴۰ نمبر) اور ابوداؤد میں میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ... ثم قال ان الله حرم على او حرم الخمر والميسر والكوبة(الف)(ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی الاوعیہ ص ۱۶۳ نمبر ۳۶۹۶) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کو بہ یعنی طبلہ جو بجانے کا ہوتا ہے وہ سب حرام ہیں۔ اور جب حرام ہیں تو ان کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال الدف حرام والمعازف حرام والكوبة حرام والمزمار حرام (ب) (سن للبیهقی ، باب ما جاء في ذم الملاحمی من المعازف والمز امیر ونحوھاج عاشر ص ۳۷۶ نمبر ۲۱۰۰۰) اس اثر سے بھی کھیل کود کی چیزیں حرام ہوئیں اس لئے ان کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کئے گا۔
And not in the cross of gold and silver, nor in chess, nor in backgammon. The cross is for the worship of Christians, which is unlawful and non-valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim). Chess and backgammon are playthings, which are not property but worthless. Therefore, stealing them does not entail cutting of the hand. The proof of their prohibition is in the verse: 'And of mankind is he who purchases idle talk to mislead from the way of Allah without knowledge and takes it in mockery; for such there will be a humiliating punishment.' (Luqman 31:6). This verse censures playthings. And regarding backgammon (nardshir), the hadith has passed: from Sulayman ibn Buraydah from his father that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever plays backgammon, it is as if he dipped his hand in the flesh and blood of swine.' (Muslim). And in Abu Dawud: from Ibn Abbas ... then he said: 'Allah has forbidden or made forbidden for me: intoxicants, gambling, and the kubah (drum).' This hadith states that the kubah (drum) and all musical instruments are forbidden. Since they are forbidden, stealing them does not entail amputation. There is an athar: from Ibn Abbas: 'The duff is forbidden, musical instruments are forbidden, the kubah is forbidden, and the flute is forbidden.' (Bayhaqi). This athar also makes playthings forbidden, so their theft does not entail amputation.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, listing three items whose theft does not incur amputation: (1) a cross made of gold or silver, (2) a chess set, (3) a backgammon set. The reason is that these items are not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in Islamic law. A cross is a symbol of disbelief and is used for worship by Christians; it has no lawful benefit, so it is not mal mutaqawwim. Chess and backgammon are frivolous playthings; they are not considered property at all, or they are of negligible value. Additionally, they are haram to play with, as evidenced by the Quranic verse condemning 'idle talk' and the hadith about backgammon equating to touching pig flesh and blood. Musical instruments like the drum (kubah) are also prohibited, as per the hadith and athar from Ibn Abbas. Since these items are not mal mutaqawwim, stealing them does not meet the condition for the hadd of amputation. The ruling is clear: the hand is not cut, although the thief may be subject to discretionary punishment (ta'zir).

Key Terms
صليب (salib): A cross, used as a symbol of Christianity; made of gold or silver.
شطرنج (shatranj): Chess; a board game played for recreation.
نرد (nard): Backgammon; a dice game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that stealing a gold cross does not entail amputation, despite the gold being valuable?
A) Because the cross is not owned by a Muslim.
B) Because the cross is not considered mal mutaqawwim due to its unlawful nature.
C) Because the nisab for gold is higher than for silver.
D) Because stealing from a non-Muslim is not subject to hadd.

Key Takeaway
The hand is not cut for stealing items that are not considered lawful property in Islam, such as a cross, chess set, or backgammon, because the condition of mal mutaqawwim is not fulfilled.

No Amputation for Stealing a Free Child or Adult Slave
Ruling with proof: no amputation for stealing a free child or adult slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing a free child or an adult slave, explain the reasoning (free human not property; adult slave can resist), and distinguish it from stealing a young slave where amputation applies.

Core Idea
A free human is never property; an adult slave can resist — both make theft of them ineligible for hadd amputation.

Hadd punishments are severe and require strict conditions. One condition is that the stolen object must be property (mal) taken clandestinely. This lesson examines two exceptions: a free child (even with ornaments) and an adult slave. The Hanafi school rules that amputation does not apply, but the thief still faces ta'zir discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Recognize that the passage covers two distinct scenarios: stealing a free child (even if wearing ornaments) and stealing an adult slave. Notice the rule: no amputation for either.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
For the free child: a free human is never considered property (mal) in Hanafi law, so stealing a person is not theft of property. For the adult slave: he can defend himself and cry out; if he does not, it implies consent, so the taking is ghasp (usurpation), not clandestine theft.
The ornaments on the child are irrelevant because the child himself is not property.
3
Note the distinction from a young slave
The passage and the athar from Sufyan clarify that stealing a young slave (who cannot defend) does incur amputation because he is property and cannot resist.
4
Recognize the source of authority
The ruling is supported by athar (narrations) from Ibn Abbas, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Umar ibn al-Khattab. These are considered qawl sahabi and serve as evidence.
The evidence is zanni al-thubut but suffices for the Hanafi position.
5
Apply the ruling
In both cases, the thief is not subject to hadd amputation but receives ta'zir (discretionary punishment). Ensure to distinguish from the case of a young slave where amputation is wajib.

And no amputation on the thief of a free child even if he has ornaments, nor on the thief of an adult slave. There is no amputation on the thief of a free child even if he has ornaments, nor on the thief of an adult slave. A free child is never property, so stealing him is like not stealing property; thus his hand is not cut, but there will be ta'zir. From Ibn Abbas concerning two men, one sold the other: He said, the sale is returned and they are punished, and there is no amputation on them. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). This athar shows that if a free person is sold or stolen, ta'zir is given, not amputation. And stealing an adult slave does not incur amputation because he can defend himself and can tell people that he was stolen; if he does not say, it is as if he consents. Even if he cries out and the thief overpowers him, it is not theft but ghasp (usurpation), and the punishment for ghasp is not amputation but execution or severe beating. Therefore, stealing an adult slave does not incur amputation. There is an athar from Sufyan: He said: 'Whoever steals a young slave (saghir mamluk), then there is amputation; whoever steals a free child or a mature slave, then there is no amputation.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). This athar states that if the slave is young, amputation applies; if free or mature slave, no amputation. And there is an athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab that he did not see amputation for them; he said: 'These are deceivers.' Our companions said: Its meaning is regarding a slave if he is intelligent. It has been narrated from Umar that he cut the hand of a man for stealing a young slave (i.e., a small slave). (Sunan al-Bayhaqi).
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, addressing two cases: (1) stealing a free child (even if wearing ornaments), and (2) stealing an adult slave. In both cases, the hand is not amputated. The reasoning: a free human, regardless of age, is not property (mal), so stealing a free person is not considered theft of property. The ornaments on the child are considered part of the child's person or are negligible for the hadd. For an adult slave, the slave has the ability to defend himself and to cry out for help. If he does not resist or call for help, it implies consent, so it is not clandestine theft but rather ghasp (usurpation), which carries a different punishment (ta'zir, possible death or severe beating). However, if the slave is young (saghir mamluk) and cannot defend himself, then stealing him does incur amputation because he is truly property and unable to resist. This distinction is supported by athar from Sufyan and the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who amputated for stealing a young slave but not for an adult slave. The passage emphasizes that the thief of a free child or adult slave receives ta'zir (discretionary punishment), not hadd.

Key Terms
صبى حر (sabi hurr): A free child; a minor who is not a slave.
عبد كبير (abd kabir): An adult slave; a slave who has reached puberty.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge, not prescribed by shar'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the hand not amputated for stealing an adult slave?
A) Because an adult slave is not considered property at all.
B) Because an adult slave can defend himself, so it is not clandestine theft.
C) Because the nisab for a slave is higher than for other property.
D) Because the thief must be a slave for amputation to apply.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, hadd amputation for theft is only for clandestine taking of property; stealing a free human or an adult slave who can resist does not meet the conditions, so only ta'zir applies.

Cutting for Stealing a Young Slave
Ruling on amputation for theft of a young slave with proof from Athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for stealing a young slave and identify the proof from the practice of the people of Madinah.

Core Idea
The hand is cut for stealing a young slave because he is valuable property, but not for an adult slave.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within the chapter of Hudud: the thief of a young male slave is subject to hadd amputation. While theft of an adult slave or subsidiary gold does not incur cutting, the young slave is considered 'mal nafis' (valuable property). The proof is an athar from the jurists of Madinah, recorded by al-Bayhaqi, confirming this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the action being ruled upon? What is the hukm (legal classification) for the thief of a young slave?
The ruling is amputation of the hand.
2
Note the exceptions
What cases are excluded from this ruling? Identify the two exceptions mentioned in the passage.
Exceptions: adult slave and subsidiary gold on the slave.
3
Examine the proof
What is the dalil (evidence) cited? Is it from the Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, or Qiyas?
The proof is an athar from the Madinan jurists, considered a type of Ijma or Sunnah.
4
Understand the rationale
Why is the young slave treated differently from an adult slave? What makes the young slave valuable property?
A young slave is considered 'mal nafis' due to his potential and economic value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۳۲] (۱۰) ويقطع سارق العبد الصغير. اس اثر سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بڑے غلام میں نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور اس پر جو سونا ہے وہ تابع ہے اس لئے وہ دس درہم سے زیادہ ہو تب بھی نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور چھوٹے غلام کے چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ اوپر اثر گزر چکا ہے اثر میں ہے۔ ثنا ابن ابی زناد عن ابيه عن الفقهاء من اهل المدينة كانوا يقولون من سرق عبدا صغيرا او اعجميا لا حيلة له قطع (الف) ( سنن بیہقی ، باب ما جاء فی من سرق عبد صغیر امن حرزج [؟] عا من ص ۴۶۵ نمبر ۱۷۲۳۰) اس سنن للبیہقی، اثر سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ چھوٹا غلام چرائے تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔
[2532] (10) And the thief of the young male slave is amputated. From this athar it is also known that for a large slave, the hand is not cut. And the gold that is on him is subsidiary, so even if it exceeds ten dirhams, it is not cut. And for the thief of a small slave, the hand is cut. The athar has passed above. In the athar: Ibn Abi Zinad narrated from his father from the jurists of Madinah: they used to say: whoever steals a small slave or a non-Arab who has no trick, he is amputated. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter: what has come about whoever steals a small slave... p. 465 no. 17230). From this athar of Bayhaqi it is also known that if one steals a small slave, the hand is cut.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that the hand of a thief who steals a young male slave ('abd saghir) is to be cut. It clarifies that if the slave is an adult (large), amputation does not apply, nor does it apply if the stolen item includes gold that is subsidiary to the slave, even if the gold exceeds the nisab. The proof is an athar from the jurists of Madinah, recorded by al-Bayhaqi, stating that whoever steals a small slave or a non-Arab who cannot defend himself is subject to amputation. This establishes that a young slave is considered valuable property (mal nafis) and thus falls under the law of theft for which the hadd punishment is prescribed. The ruling is consistent with the general Hanafi position that the stolen item must be a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) to incur the hadd.

Key Terms
عبد صغير ('abd saghir): A young male slave who is not yet an adult, considered a valuable property in the context of theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The hand is cut because a young slave is valuable property.
C) Only the gold on the slave is considered, not the slave itself.
D) The hand is cut only if the slave is an adult.

Key Takeaway
A young slave is considered valuable property (mal nafis) such that stealing him incurs the hadd punishment of amputation, as established by the practice of the Madinan jurists.

Theft of Registers: Amputation Only for Accounting Books
Ruling on amputation for theft of registers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for theft of registers and identify the condition of valuable property (mal nafis) that triggers hadd.

Core Idea
Only accounting books are considered valuable property; ordinary registers do not incur hadd punishment.

This passage clarifies a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding theft of registers. While theft generally incurs hadd if certain conditions are met, the jurists specify that not every stolen item triggers amputation. Here, the criterion of value (mal nafis) is applied to differentiate between ordinary registers and accounting books. Understanding this distinction helps in applying the general principle of hadd to various stolen items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on theft
Recall that Hanafi fiqh imposes amputation (hadd) for theft only when the stolen item is valuable property (mal nafis) and meets other conditions like nisab.
This background is necessary to understand the exception.
2
Locate the exception in the passage
The passage explicitly states: 'No amputation in all registers except accounting books.' Identify the two categories: ordinary registers and accounting books.
The exception is based on value.
3
Analyze the reason for the distinction
The commentary explains that ordinary registers lack importance to be called valuable property, while accounting books contain accounts and are considered valuable and excellent. Thus, the hadd applies only to the latter.
Value is determined by content and utility, not just physical form.
4
Apply the principle to other items
Consider other similar items: a diary, a cash book, a ledger. Would they be considered valuable? Apply the same criterion: Does the item hold significant financial information or intrinsic worth?
This step tests understanding of the underlying condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۳۳] (۱۱) ولا قطع في الدفاتر كلها الا في دفاتر الحساب. ہاتھ کاٹنا نہیں ہے کسی دفتر کے چرانے میں سوائے حساب کے دفتر کے۔ حساب کے علاوہ کے دفتر اور رجسٹر کی اہمیت زیادہ نہیں ہوتی کہ اس کو نفیس مال کہا جائے۔ اس لئے اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور حساب کار جسر البتہ نفیس اور عمدہ سمجھا جاتا ہے، کیونکہ اس میں حساب ہے۔ اس لئے اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔
[2533] (11) And there is no amputation in all registers except in registers of accounting. There is no hand cutting for stealing any register except the register of accounting. Registers other than accounting do not have much importance to be called valuable property. Therefore, for stealing them, the hand is not cut. And the accounting book is indeed considered valuable and excellent, because it contains accounts. Therefore, for stealing it, the hand is cut.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn stating that amputation does not apply to the theft of any registers (dafatir) except for accounting books (dafatir al-hisab). The commentator explains that ordinary registers lack the significance to be considered valuable property (mal nafis) in the sight of Shari'ah, so the hadd punishment does not apply. Accounting books, on the other hand, are considered valuable because they contain financial records, thus their theft incurs amputation. This ruling is based on the general principle that the stolen item must be a valuable property for the hadd to be imposed.

Key Terms
دفاتر (dafatir): Registers, records, or books used for writing, specifically excluding accounting books in terms of value.
دفاتر الحساب (dafatir al-hisab): Accounting books containing financial accounts, considered valuable property for theft ruling.
مال نفيس (mal nafis): Valuable property; an essential condition for applying hadd in theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because an ordinary register is not a book.
B) Because an accounting book is considered valuable property.
C) Because all books are valuable according to Shari'ah.
D) Because the nisab for theft is higher for ordinary registers.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling restricts amputation for theft of registers only to accounting books, based on the condition that the stolen item must be valuable property (mal nafis).

No Amputation for Stealing Impure or Prohibited Items
Ruling excluding impure and prohibited items from theft amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of impure and prohibited items and identify the evidence and underlying principle that excludes them from hadd al-sariqa.

Core Idea
Shari’ah does not protect impure or prohibited items with hadd.

In the Hanafi school, the punishment of hand amputation for theft is reserved for stealing valuable property (mal nafis). This lesson explains why items like dogs, cheetahs, and musical instruments are exempt, even if they have market value. We will explore the hadith evidence and the principle that Shari’ah considers certain items legally insignificant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Items
Read the heading and the initial statement. List the five items for which amputation is not applied: dog, cheetah, tambourine, drum, flute.
Note that 'mizmar' represents wind instruments generally.
2
Understand the Condition for Hadd
The underlying condition for hadd is that the stolen item must be 'mal nafis' – valuable property from a Shari’ah perspective. Items that are impure (najis) or prohibited (haram) are not considered valuable, even if they have monetary worth in markets.
This is a key Hanafi principle: purity and permissibility affect property status.
3
Examine the Evidence for Each Category
The passage cites four ahadith. The first proves dogs and cheetahs are impure (commanded to kill dogs, forbids eating fanged beasts). The second proves drums are haram (kubah = drum). The third proves musical instruments are haram (ma'azif).
Note that the hadith on dogs also covers cheetahs by analogy (qiyas).
4
Apply the General Principle
The concluding sentence states the principle: 'Whatever Shari’ah considers insignificant (haqir) does not qualify for hadd; forbidden things are inherently insignificant.' This is the decisive criterion.
This principle is not limited to these five items – it applies to all similarly impure or prohibited property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۳۴] (۱۲) ولا يقطع سارق كلب ولا فهد ولا دف ولا طبل ولا مزمار. اور کتے کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا اور نہ چیتے اور نہ دف اور نہ ڈھول اور نہ سارنگی کے چرانے میں ہاتھ کاٹا جائیگا۔ کتانا پاک جانور ہے، اسی طرح چیتا نا پاک جانور ہے اس لئے وہ نفیس چیز نہیں رہی اس لئے اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن جابر قال امر نبی الله الا الله بقتل الكلاب حتى ان كانت المرأة تقدم من البادية يعنى بالكلب فنقتله ثم نهانا عن قتلها وقال عليكم بالاسود (ب) (ابو داؤد شریف، باب اتخاذ الكلب للصيد وغیرہ ص ۳۷ نمبر ۲۸۴۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کتے کو قتل کرنے کا حکم ہے اس لئے اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا اور یہی حکم چیتا اور شیر کا بھی ہے۔ ناپاکی کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابی ثعلبة ان رسول الله الا الله نهى عن اكل كل ذي ناب من السباع (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب اکل کل ذی ناب من السباع ص ۱۳۰ نمبر ۵۵۳۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ پھاڑ کھانے والے جانور کا گوشت نا پاک ہے اس لئے معمولی چیز ہوگئی۔ دف ، ڈھول اور سارنگی کے ناجائز ہونے کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ... ان الله حرم على او حرم الخمر والميسر والكوبة (و) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب في الاوعية ص ۶۳ نمبر ۳۶۹۶) کو بتہ کا معنی ڈھول ہے اس لئے ڈھول بھی حرام ہوا۔ سارنگی کے سلسلے میں بخاری کی لمبی حدیث کا ٹکڑا ہے۔ حدثنی ابو عامر الاشعرى ... سمع النبي الله يقول ليكونن من امتى اقوام يستحلون الحر والحرير والخمر والمعازف (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب ما جاء فيمن يستحل الخمر ويسميه بغير اسمه ص ۱۸۳۷ نمبر ۵۵۹۰) اس حدیث میں معازف کے حرام ہونے کا تذکرہ ہے۔ اس لئے ان کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں جائے گا۔ صول یہ مسئلے اس اصول پر ہیں کہ جو چیز شریعت کی نگاہ میں معمولی ہے اس کے چرانے سے ہاتھ نہیں کئے گا، اور حرام چیزیں شریعت کی نگاہ میں معمولی ہیں اس لئے اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ نہیں کٹے گا۔
[2534] (12) And the thief of a dog, a cheetah, a tambourine, a drum, or a flute is not amputated. And the hand is not cut for stealing a dog, nor a cheetah, nor a tambourine, nor a drum, nor a sarangi. The dog is an impure animal, similarly the cheetah is an impure animal, so it is not a valuable thing; therefore, for stealing it, the hand is not cut. There is a hadith: Jabir said: The Prophet commanded the killing of dogs until a woman from the desert would come with a dog and we would kill it, then he forbade us from killing them and said: just kill the black one. (Abu Dawud, chapter on keeping dogs for hunting etc., p. 37 no. 2846). From this hadith it is known that there is a command to kill dogs, so for stealing them the hand is not cut, and the same ruling applies to cheetah and lion. The proof of impurity is the hadith: from Abu Tha'laba that the Prophet forbade eating every beast of prey with fangs. (Bukhari, chapter on eating every beast of prey with fangs, p. 130 no. 5530). From this hadith it is known that the meat of predatory animals is impure, so it becomes an insignificant thing. The proof of the impermissibility of tambourine, drum, and sarangi is the hadith: from Ibn Abbas ... that Allah forbade, or the Prophet forbade, wine, gambling, and al-kubah. (Abu Dawud, chapter on vessels, p. 63 no. 3696). Al-kubah means drum, so the drum is also forbidden. Regarding the sarangi, there is a piece of a long hadith in Bukhari: Abu Amir al-Ash'ari narrated... he heard the Prophet say: There will surely be people from my nation who consider permissible (fornication?), silk, wine, and musical instruments. (Bukhari, chapter on those who consider wine permissible and call it by other names, p. 1837 no. 5590). In this hadith, musical instruments are mentioned as forbidden. Therefore, for stealing them, the hand will not be cut. The summary: these issues are based on the principle that whatever is considered insignificant in the view of Shari'ah, its theft does not incur hand amputation, and forbidden things are considered insignificant in Shari'ah, so their theft does not incur amputation.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage lists items whose theft does not incur hand amputation: dogs, cheetahs, tambourines (daff), drums (tabl), and flutes or musical instruments (mizmar, sarangi). The reasoning is that these items are not considered valuable property (mal nafis) in Shari'ah. Dogs and cheetahs are impure animals (najis), as evidenced by hadith commanding the killing of dogs and the prohibition of eating beasts of prey with fangs. Musical instruments are prohibited (haram) as indicated by ahadith that mention the prohibition of musical instruments and drums. The general principle stated is that whatever Shari'ah considers insignificant (haqir) does not qualify for the hadd of theft, and forbidden items are inherently insignificant. Therefore, amputation is not prescribed for stealing these items.

Key Terms
كلب (kalb): Dog, an animal considered impure and not valuable property for theft purposes.
فهد (fahd): Cheetah, a predatory animal considered impure like dogs.
دف (duff): Tambourine, a musical instrument considered impermissible (haram).
طبل (tabl): Drum, a musical instrument considered impermissible.
مزمار (mizmar): Flute or any wind instrument, considered impermissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Theft of impure or prohibited items does not incur hadd al-sariqa because they are not considered valuable property in Shari'ah.

Amputation for Stealing Valuable Wood
Ruling that theft of valuable types of wood incurs hand amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that stealing teak, lancewood, ebony, and sandalwood incurs hadd amputation, and identify the condition of value that distinguishes these woods from ordinary timber.

Core Idea
Stealing valuable wood like teak, ebony, and sandalwood incurs hand amputation.

Not all wood is equal in Islamic law. While ordinary timber may not meet the threshold for the hadd penalty, certain prized woods are considered valuable property (mal qimati). This lesson clarifies which specific woods trigger the full penalty of hand amputation under Hanafi fiqh and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage lists four types of wood: teak, lancewood, ebony, and sandalwood. The ruling is that stealing any of these incurs hand amputation (hadd).
2
Recognize the Key Condition
The commentary explains that these woods are valuable (qimati). In Hanafi fiqh, for hadd to apply, the stolen item must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and above the nisab threshold.
Value is not merely market price but legal recognition as valuable property.
3
Contrast with Ordinary Wood
Ordinary woods like pine or oak, even if worth the nisab, may not be considered 'valuable' in the same sense. The hadd is specifically affirmed for these enumerated precious woods.
Some later Hanafi scholars extend the ruling to any wood valued as property.
4
Apply the Concept
If a person steals a piece of teak furniture, check whether the wood itself is the target and whether it is considered valuable. If so, amputation applies.
Other conditions of hadd (e.g., hiraz, no joint ownership) must also be met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۳۵] (۱۳) ويُقطع في الساج والقناء والآبنوس والصندل. اور کاٹا جائے گا ساگون، نیزے کی لکڑی، ابنوس اور صندل کی لکڑی چرانے میں۔ یہ لکڑیاں قیمتی ہیں اس لئے ان کے چرانے میں ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔
[2535] (13) And amputation is done for [stealing] teak, lancewood, ebony, and sandalwood. And the hand is cut for stealing teak, spear wood, ebony, and sandalwood. These woods are valuable, so for stealing them, the hand is cut.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This ruling states that amputation applies to the theft of certain types of wood: teak (saj), lancewood or spear wood (qana'), ebony (abnus), and sandalwood (sandali). The commentator explains that these woods are valuable (qimati), meaning they are considered valuable property (mal nafis) in Shari'ah, and therefore their theft incurs the hadd of hand amputation. This contrasts with ordinary wood, which may not be valuable enough to warrant hadd.

Key Terms
ساج (saj): Teak wood, a valuable type of timber.
قناء (qana'): Lancewood or spear-wood, a sturdy valuable wood.
آبنوس (abnus): Ebony, a dense black valuable wood.
صندل (sandali): Sandalwood, a fragrant valuable wood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Theft of teak, lancewood, ebony, and sandalwood incurs hand amputation because they are considered valuable property in Hanafi fiqh.

Amputation for Stealing Manufactured Wooden Items
Ruling that ordinary wood becomes valuable when made into vessels or doors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for theft of manufactured wooden vessels or doors and explain why manufacturing transforms the value.

Core Idea
Manufacturing ordinary wood into a vessel or door raises its value, making its theft subject to hadd amputation.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri addresses a key nuance in hudud: the value of stolen property is assessed at the time of theft. Ordinary wood may not warrant amputation, but once it is made into a vessel or door, its classification changes. Understanding this transformation is crucial for correctly applying the hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: theft of wooden vessels or doors. The ruling is that amputation (cutting of the hand) is applied.
2
Identify the condition
Recognize that the condition for amputation is that the wood must be manufactured into a vessel or door. Raw ordinary wood does not trigger amputation.
3
Explain the legal reasoning
Understand that manufacturing transforms ordinary wood into valuable property. The item is no longer ordinary wood but a valuable finished product, meeting the nisab requirement for hadd.
The thief is charged with stealing a vessel or door, not wood.
4
Note the practical implication
In practice, the value of the item at the time of theft is decisive. If raw material is stolen, then later manufactured by the thief, the hadd does not apply because the theft occurred before the value transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۳۷] (۱۴) واذا اتخذ من الخشب اوانی او ابواب قطع فيها. اگر لکڑی سے برتن بنایا، دروازے بنائے تو ان میں ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ عام لکڑی تھی جس کے چرانے سے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جاتا لیکن اس سے برتن بنالیا یا دروازہ بنالیا تو اب اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ کا ٹا جائیگا۔ اب یہ معمولی نہیں رہی بلکہ قیمتی ہو گئی اس لئے یوں کہا جائے گا کہ برتن چرایا یا دروازہ چرایا۔ اس لئے اس کے چرانے میں ہاتھ کا نا جائیگا۔ اوانی : جمع ہے آمیہ کی برتن، ابواب : جمع ہے باب کی کی دروازہ۔
[2537] (14) And when vessels or doors are made from the wood, amputation is done for them. If vessels are made from wood, or doors are made, then the hand is cut for them. It was ordinary wood for which stealing would not incur hand cutting, but if vessels or doors are made from it, then now for stealing it the hand will be cut. Now it is no longer ordinary but has become valuable, so it will be said that he stole a vessel or a door. Therefore, for stealing it, the hand will be cut. 'Awani' is the plural of 'inya' meaning vessel; 'abwab' is plural of 'bab' meaning door.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This ruling addresses the scenario where ordinary wood, which is not valuable enough to incur hadd when stolen, is transformed into vessels (awani) or doors (abwab). After manufacturing, the wood becomes a valuable finished product. The tariff has changed: it is now a vessel or door, not just wood. Therefore, theft of such items incurs hand amputation. This demonstrates that the criterion for amputation is the value of the item at the time of theft, not the raw material's original value.

Key Terms
اوانی (awani): Plural of inya: vessels, utensils made from wood.
ابواب (abwab): Plural of bab: doors, considered valuable property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because doors are always made of valuable wood.
D) Because theft of any manufactured item incurs hadd.

Key Takeaway
Manufacturing ordinary wood into a vessel or door makes it valuable property, so its theft incurs hadd amputation.

No Amputation for Breach of Trust (Khiyanah)
Ruling that amputation is not prescribed for breach of trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyanah, explain why amputation is not applied, and identify the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A trustee who embezzles is not a thief — no amputation, only ta'zir.

Breach of trust (khiyanah) involves taking property that was lawfully in one's custody. This differs fundamentally from theft (sariq), which requires stealth from a secure place. The Hanafi school, relying on a clear hadith, holds that amputation is not prescribed for khiyanah. Instead, the judge applies a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). Let's explore the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is khiyanah (breach of trust) — a person entrusted with property embezzles it. The ruling is that the hadd of hand amputation does not apply.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith narrated by Jabir ibn Abdullah: 'There is no amputation upon a treacherous person, nor upon a plunderer, nor upon a usurper.' This is recorded by Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, and Nasa'i.
The hadith is ahad (non-mutawatir) but its indicatory meaning (dalala) is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly negates amputation for the treacherous person ('لا قطع على خائن'). The word 'خائن' is general and includes both male and female.
4
Identify the underlying rationale
The commentary explains that theft requires stealthy removal from a secure place (hirz). In khiyanah, the trustee already has lawful access, so no stealth is involved. This missing element makes hadd inapplicable.
5
Note the alternative punishment
Instead of hadd, the judge imposes ta'zir — a discretionary punishment that can include fines, imprisonment, or lashing, depending on the circumstances.
Ta'zir ensures accountability without violating the strict conditions of hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۳۷] (۱۵) ولا قطع على خائن ولا خائنة. خیانت کرنے والے مرد اور خیانت کرنے والی عورت پر کاٹنا نہیں ہے۔ کسی آدمی کے پاس امانت کی رقم تھی یا مال تھا اس نے اس میں خیانت کر لی تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ البتہ تعزیر کی جائے گی۔ اس میں چوری کا معنی نہیں پایا گیا ، چوری کہتے ہیں محفوظ جگہ سے چپکے سے کسی مال کو اٹھا کر لے جانا۔ اور خیانت میں چپکے سے اٹھانا نہیں پایا گیا اس لئے نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن جابر عن النبي الله قال ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس صلى الله عليه وسلم قطع (ب) ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی الخائن والمشکلس و المنتهب ص ۲۶۸ نمبر ۱۴۴۸ رابوداؤد شریف، باب القطع في النخلستة والخياني ص ۲۵۵، نمبر ۴۳۹۲/۱۴۳۹ رنسائی شریف نمبر ۴۹۷۱) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ خیانت کرنے والے کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
[2537] (15) And there is no amputation upon a treacherous man or a treacherous woman. There is no hand cutting for a man who commits khiyanah or a woman who commits khiyanah. If a person had an amanah (trust) of money or property and he committed khiyanah in it, then his hand is not cut; rather he is given ta'zir. In this, the meaning of theft is not found. Theft is called: taking away property stealthily from a protected place. And in khiyanah, stealthy taking is not found, so it is not cut. And there is a hadith: Jabir narrated from the Prophet said: There is no amputation upon a treacherous person, nor upon a plunderer, nor upon a usurper. (Tirmidhi, chapter on the treacherous and usurper and plunderer, p. 268 no. 1448; Abu Dawud, chapter on amputation in robbery and treachery, p. 255 no. 4392/1439; Nasa'i, no. 4971). From this hadith it is known that the hand of the treacherous is not cut.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage rules that the punishable act of breach of trust (khiyanah) does not incur hand amputation. The thief (sariq) is defined as one who stealthily takes property from a secure place (hirz). A trustee who embezzles does not take stealthily from a secure place because the property is already in his custody. Therefore, the hadd does not apply. Instead, the offender is subject to ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The proof is a hadith from Jabir that explicitly excludes the treacherous person, plunderer, and usurper from amputation. This distinguishes theft from other forms of wrongful taking of property.

Key Terms
خائن (kha'in): A person who commits breach of trust (khiyanah) by embezzling property entrusted to him.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Breach of trust (khiyanah) does not meet the strict definition of theft (sariq) requiring stealth from a secure place, hence hand amputation is not applied; the offender receives ta'zir.

Amputation for Theft of Valuable Woods
Ruling on hand amputation for theft of four valuable woods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four valuable woods mentioned in the text and explain why their theft triggers the hadd punishment of hand amputation.

Core Idea
Theft of these four valuable woods incurs the hadd punishment of hand amputation.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school on the hudud punishment for theft. The passage states that the hand is amputated for stealing saj (teak), qana' (spear-wood), abnos (ebony), and sandalwood. The key point is that these woods are considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), so the full hadd applies. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the condition of value in theft cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its punishment
The act is the theft of specific woods, and the punishment is hand amputation (qat' al-yad). Recognize that this is a fixed punishment from Allah (hadd).
2
List the four woods
Memorize the four types: saj (teak), qana' (spear-wood), abnos (ebony), sandal (sandalwood). These are all types of valuable timber.
The woods are valuable due to their durability, fragrance, or use in valuable items.
3
Understand the underlying condition
The common factor is that these woods are considered mal mutaqawwim (valuable property). Theft of non-valuable wood (e.g., ordinary firewood) does not incur amputation.
This condition is derived from general Hanafi principles on theft.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, if someone steals a piece of teak worth the nisab (minimum value for theft), the hand is amputated. If the wood is not valuable, the punishment is discretionary (ta'zir).
The nisab for theft is 10 dirhams or equivalent in the Hanafi school.

No Amputation for the Treacherous (Kha'in)
Ruling excluding treacherous persons from hand amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a treacherous person (kha'in) and explain why hadd amputation does not apply.

Core Idea
Breach of trust lacks stealth; without stealth and hirz, there is no theft, so no hadd.

In Islamic criminal law, the hadd punishment of hand amputation is reserved for the specific crime of theft (sariqah). This lesson examines a key exception: a person who betrays a trust (khiyanah) does not face amputation. The reason lies in the legal definition of theft, which requires secret removal from a guarded place. The Prophet's explicit hadith confirms this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is khiyanah (breach of trust). The ruling is that there is no hadd amputation; instead, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is applied.
2
Understand the exclusionary condition
The passage explains that theft requires two elements: secrecy and removal from a secure place (hirz). Khiyanah lacks the element of stealth because the property is already lawfully in the person's possession.
Hanafi definition of theft: al-sariqah = akhdh al-mal min al-hirz khufyat.
3
Examine the textual proof
The hadith from Jabir (ra) explicitly excludes the kha'in, the plunderer (munthahib), and the snatcher (mukhtalis) from amputation. This is a direct dalil for the ruling.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qati' al-dalalah).
4
Distinguish from theft
Apply the Hanafi definition: sariqah = taking from hirz secretly. Khiyanah involves property entrusted to the person, so no unlawful taking from hirz occurs.
If a person entrusted with a key uses it to steal from a locked room, it is still khiyanah, not theft, because the access was lawful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قطع على خائن ولا خائنة (15) خیانت کرنے والے مرد اور خیانت کرنے والی عورت پر کاٹنا نہیں ہے۔ کسی آدمی کے پاس امانت کی رقم تھی یا مال تھا اس نے اس میں خیانت کر لی تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ البتہ تعزیر کی جائے گی۔ اس میں چوری کا معنی نہیں پایا گیا ، چوری کہتے ہیں محفوظ جگہ سے چپکے سے کسی مال کو اٹھا کر لے جانا۔ اور خیانت میں چپکے سے اٹھانا نہیں پایا گیا اس لئے نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن جابر عن النبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس قطع.
And there is no amputation for a treacherous man or a treacherous woman. (15) There is no cutting for a treacherous man or a treacherous woman. A person had an entrusted sum or property and he acted treacherously with it, then his hand will not be cut. Rather, he will be given ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The meaning of theft is not found in this, for theft is to secretly take property from a secure place. And in treachery, secret taking is not found, so the hand will not be cut. In a hadith: from Jabir from the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: There is no amputation for a treacherous person, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling (hukm) that a person who betrays a trust (kha'in) does not have the hand amputated, even if they take property that was entrusted to them. The commentary explains that this is because the act does not constitute theft (sariqah) in the legal sense: theft requires taking secretly from a guarded place (hirz). Treachery involves property that is already in the person's possession lawfully, so the element of stealthy removal from protection is missing. The proof is a hadith narrated by Jabir (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) declared that there is no amputation for a treacherous person, a plunderer, or a snatcher. The punishment for khiyanah is discretionary (ta'zir), not hadd.

Key Terms
خائن (kha'in): A person who betrays a trust or commits breach of trust (khiyanah) with property.
تعزیر (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses not subject to hadd or qisas.
حرز (hirz): A secure, guarded place for property; theft requires taking from hirz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the taking is not secret and not from a guarded place

D) Because the hadith only mentions men

Key Takeaway
Only theft from a secure place with stealth incurs hadd amputation; breach of trust is punished by ta'zir.

No Amputation for Grave Robber, Plunderer, or Snatcher
Ruling with dispute on exemption from amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that no hadd amputation applies to the nabbash (grave robber), muntahib (plunderer), and mukhtalis (snatcher), and evaluate the counter-argument from Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Grave robbery, plunder, and snatching are not theft from a guarded place — no amputation, only ta'zir.

The hadd penalty for theft (sariqa) requires specific conditions: the property must be taken from a guarded place (hirz) and above a minimum value. This lesson examines three categories of takers who are exempted from amputation under Hanafi law: the one who steals a shroud from a grave (nabbash), the one who openly plunders (muntahib), and the one who snatches by trickery (mukhtalis). The passage provides the ruling and evidence, then presents a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf, who argue for amputation in the case of grave robbery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types of offenders: nabbash (grave robber), muntahib (plunderer), and mukhtalis (snatcher). Understand the distinguishing feature of each: none involves secret theft from a secured location.
The common thread is the absence of a valid hirz.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that none of these three undergo hadd amputation; instead they receive ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
Examine the athar from al-Zuhri, showing Companions' consensus on ta'zir not amputation, and the hadith of Jabir exempting khain, muntahib, and mukhtalis. Also note the two reasons: graveyard is not hirz, and shroud becomes trivial after burial.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalala according to the text.
4
Understand the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf argue that the grave robber is a thief by analogy to theft from the living, citing a statement of al-Sha'bi.
Note that Abu Yusuf's view is an intra-Hanafi divergence.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: in a Hanafi jurisdiction, the nabbash receives ta'zir; in a Shafi'i or Abu Yusuf jurisdiction, he would be amputated. The majority Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.
This highlights how a subtle difference in analogy leads to a major penal difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نباش و لا منتهب ولا مختلس (16) اور نہ کفن چور پر ہاتھ کاٹنا ہے نہ لٹیرے پر ، نہ اچھکے پر۔ جو آدمی کفن چراتا ہو یا جو لوٹ کر سب کے سامنے سے لے جاتا ہو یا چکمہ سے مال لے لیتا ہو ان کے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹے جائیں گے۔ کفن چرانے والا مقام محفوظ سے نہیں چراتا کیونکہ قبرستان مقام محفوظ نہیں ہے۔ دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ مردہ پر کفن ڈال دینے کے بعد وہ معمولی اور گھٹیا قسم کی چیز بھی جاتی ہے۔ اس لئے کفن چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن الزهري قال اخذ نباش في زمان معاوية كان مروان على المدينة فسأل من بحضرته من اصحاب رسول الله بالمدينة والفقهاء فلم يجدوا احدا قطعه قال فاجمع رأيهم على ان يضربه ويطاف به. امام شافعی اور امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ کفن چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ عن الشعبي قال النباش سارق دوسری روایت میں ہے قال يقطع فی امواتنا كما يقطع في احيائنا.
And there is no amputation for a grave robber, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher. (16) And the hand is not cut for a grave robber, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher. A person who steals a shroud, or who seizes openly, or who takes property by trickery, their hands will not be cut. The grave robber does not steal from a guarded place because the graveyard is not a guarded place. Second, after the shroud is placed on the dead, it becomes a trivial and lowly item. Therefore the hand of the grave robber is not cut. In an athar: from al-Zuhri, he said: A grave robber was caught in the time of Mu'awiyah, and Marwan was governor of Madinah, so he asked those present of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah in Madinah and the jurists, and he did not find anyone who cut [the hand]; he said, they all agreed that he should be beaten and paraded. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf say that the hand of the grave robber is cut. From al-Sha'bi: The grave robber is a thief; in another narration: He said: The hand is cut for [stealing from] our dead just as it is cut for [stealing from] our living.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with a dispute. The Hanafi position, based on the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him) narrated by Jabir and the practice of the Companions, is that the hand is not amputated for three categories: (1) al-nabbash (grave robber who steals the shroud from the dead), (2) al-muntahib (plunderer who takes openly by force), and (3) al-mukhtalis (snatcher who takes by trickery). For the grave robber, the commentary provides two reasons: the graveyard is not a guarded place (hirz), and the shroud becomes trivial after burial. Evidence is given from the athar of al-Zuhri about the time of Mu'awiyah where the Companions and jurists agreed on ta'zir, not amputation. However, Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) hold that the hand is cut for grave robbery, citing the statement of al-Sha'bi that the grave robber is a thief and analogizing to theft from the living. The mukhtalaf fih is between the majority of Hanafis (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and these two scholars.

Key Terms
نباش (nabbash): A person who steals the shroud (kafan) from a grave.
منتهب (muntahib): A plunderer who openly takes property by force.
مختلس (mukhtalis): A snatcher who takes property by trickery or deceit.
تعزیر (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses not reaching hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the punishment for grave robbery is death, not amputation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts the grave robber, plunderer, and snatcher from hadd amputation due to the absence of a guarded place (hirz), applying ta'zir instead.

No Amputation for Theft from Public Treasury or Shared Property
Ruling exempting thief from hadd in two cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from Bayt al-Mal and shared property, and explain the principle of shubha that drops the hadd.

Core Idea
If the thief has any ownership share, hadd amputation is dropped.

Theft is a major sin, but the hadd punishment of hand amputation is not applied in every case. This lesson examines two specific exceptions: stealing from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal) and from jointly owned property. The Hanafi school holds that hadd is averted by shubha (legal doubt), and partial ownership creates such a doubt. Instead, the thief receives ta'zir (discretionary punishment).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage mentions two scenarios: theft from Bayt al-Mal and theft from property where the thief has a share.
2
State the ruling for each
In both cases, the hand is not amputated (la yuqta').
3
Understand the underlying principle
The principle is that if the thief has any portion (shirkah) in the property, the hadd falls. This is because of shubha.
Shubha means doubt, which prevents the application of hadd.
4
Relate to Bayt al-Mal
Every Muslim has a share in the public treasury, so stealing from it includes taking one's own portion, creating shubha.
5
Recognize the consequence
Though hadd is dropped, the act is still haram, and the thief is punished with ta'zir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُقطع السارق من بيت المال ولا من المال الذي فيه شركة (17) بیت المال سے چرانے والے کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا اور نہ اس مال میں جس میں چور کی شرکت ہو۔ کوئی آدمی بیت المال سے چوری کرے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یا چور کا بھی مال تھا اور دوسرے کا بھی مال ساتھ میں تھا اس میں سے چور نے چوری کر لی تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ کسی مال میں چور کا کچھ بھی حصہ ہو تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
And the thief from the public treasury is not amputated, nor from property in which there is a share. (17) The hand of a thief from Bayt al-Mal will not be cut, nor from that property in which the thief has a share. If a person steals from Bayt al-Mal, his hand will not be cut. Or if the thief had his own property and another's property together, and he steals part of it, his hand will not be cut. This ruling is based on the principle that if the thief has any portion in the property, the hand is not cut.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling that hand amputation is not applied to a thief who steals from Bayt al-Mal (the public treasury) or from property in which he himself has a share (shirkah). The commentary explains the underlying principle: if the thief has any ownership right (milk) in the property, however small, the hadd amputation is not applicable because the element of taking someone else's property exclusively is not fully present. The principle is rooted in the Hanafi understanding that hadd is warded off by doubts (shubha), and shared ownership creates a doubt. The thief from Bayt al-Mal is also considered to have a share by virtue of being a Muslim and thus a beneficiary of the treasury. Therefore, only ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is imposed.

Key Terms
بیت المال (Bayt al-Mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state; property owned by the community.
شرکت (shirkah): Partnership or shared ownership in property.
شبهہ (shubha): Doubt or legal ambiguity that prevents application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Exemption of Grave Robber, Plunderer, and Swindler from Hand Amputation
States that three types of thieves are exempt from hand amputation with evidence and disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school exempts the grave robber, plunderer, and swindler from hand amputation, and evaluate the opposing proof.

Core Idea
Secret taking from a secure place is essential for theft; open plunder, swindle, and grave robbing lack it.

This lesson examines three categories of takers who are not subject to the hadd of amputation in Hanafi law. The grave robber (al-nabbash), the plunderer (al-muntahib), and the swindler (al-mukhtalis) each violate property rights but lack a key condition: secret taking (ikhfa') from a secure place (hirz). The Hanafi school distinguishes theft (sarqah) from these other forms of unlawful taking, supported by prophetic hadith and companion consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exempt categories
State who they are: nabbash (grave robber), muntahib (plunderer), mukhtalis (swindler).
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its conditions
No amputation for any of them. Conditions for theft: secret taking from a secure place (hirz).
The grave robber lacks hirz; plunderer and swindler lack secrecy.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof for the grave robber
Athar of al-Zuhri: Companions did not cut the hand of a grave robber; they beat him and paraded him.
This indicates ijma' of the Companions.
4
Analyze the opposing proof (Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf)
Narration from al-Sha'bi: 'The grave robber is a thief' and 'He is cut among our dead as he is cut among our living.'
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The hadith of Jabir explicitly exempts plunderer and swindler; by analogy, grave robber also lacks hirz. Companion consensus overrides isolated narration.
Mufta bihi: no amputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نباش و لا منتهب ولا مختلس

(۱۶) اور نہ کفن چور پر ہاتھ کاٹنا ہے نہ لٹیرے پر ، نہ اچھکے پر۔ جو آدمی کفن چراتا ہو یا جو لوٹ کر سب کے سامنے سے لے جاتا ہو یا چکمہ سے مال لے لیتا ہو ان کے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹے جائیں گے۔ کفن چرانے والا مقام محفوظ سے نہیں چراتا کیونکہ قبرستان مقام محفوظ نہیں ہے۔ دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ مردہ پر کفن ڈال دینے کے بعد وہ معمولی اور گھٹیا قسم کی چیز بھی جاتی ہے۔ اس لئے کفن چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن الزهري قال اخذ نباش في زمان معاوية كان مروان على المدينة فسأل من بحضرته من اصحاب رسول الله بالمدينة والفقهاء فلم يجدوا احدا قطعه قال فاجمع رأيهم على ان يضربه ويطاف به (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبہ ۸۹ ماجاء فی النباش یوخذ ما حده؟ ج خامس، ص ۵۱۸ نمبر ۲۸۶۰۴) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کفن چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔

امام شافعی اور امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ کفن چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔

عن الشعبي قال النباش سارق دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ قال يقطع فی امواتنا كما يقطع في احيائنا (ب) (سنن ) باب النباش يقطع اذا اخرج الكفن من جميع القمر ج ثامن ص ۴۶۸ نمبر ۷۲۴۱/۱۷۲۳۹ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۸۹ ماجاء فی النباش یوخذ ماحده؟ ج خامس ص ۵۱۸ نمبر ۶ ۲۸۶۰) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کفن چور کا ہاتھ کا ٹا جائے گا۔ اور لٹیرے اور اچکے کے بارے میں حدیث گزرچکی ہے۔ عن جابر عن النبی صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس قطع (ج) ( ترندی شریف ، باب ماجاء فی الخائن و اشکلس و المنتصب ص ۲۶۸ نمبر ۱۴۴۸را بودا و دشریف، نمبر ۴۳۹۳/۴۳۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو آدمی دھللے کے ساتھ لوٹ کر لے جاتا ہو یا چکما دے کر اچک لے جاتا ہو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) دھللے کے ساتھ لوٹ کر لے جانے میں چپکے سے لے جانا جو چوری کا معنی ہے نہیں پایا گیا۔ اسی طرح سامنے چکما دے کر لے گیا تو چپکے سے لے جانا نہیں پایا گیا اس لئے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
And not the grave robber, nor the plunderer, nor the swindler. (16) And not is the grave robber's hand cut, nor the plunderer's, nor the swindler's. A person who steals a shroud, or who takes by plunder openly, or who takes by trickery, their hands are not cut. The grave robber does not steal from a secure place because the graveyard is not a secure place. Secondly, after the shroud is placed on the deceased, it becomes a lowly item. Therefore the grave robber's hand is not cut. In the athar: from al-Zuhri: 'A grave robber was caught in the time of Mu'awiyah, and Marwan was governor of Medina. He asked the Companions of the Messenger of Allah and the jurists who were present, and none of them said to cut. He said: their consensus was to beat him and parade him around.' (Ibn Abi Shaybah). From this athar it is learned that the grave robber's hand is not cut. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf say: the grave robber's hand is cut. From al-Sha'bi: 'The grave robber is a thief.' In another narration: 'He is cut among our dead as he is cut among our living.' (Sunan, Bab al-Nabbash). From this athar it is learned that the grave robber's hand is cut. And the hadith about the plunderer and swindler has passed: from Jabir from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'There is no cutting for a betrayer, nor a plunderer, nor a swindler.' (Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud). From this hadith it is learned that the person who takes by force or by trickery, his hand is not cut. (2) In taking by force, the secret taking which is the meaning of theft is not found. Similarly, if he takes by trickery publicly, secret taking is not found, so his hand is not cut.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the grave robber (al-nabbash), and also states the ruling for the plunderer (al-muntahib) and swindler (al-mukhtalis). The Hanafi position is that none of these are subject to hand amputation (al-qat'). The grave robber is not cut because the graveyard is not a secure place (hirz), as security is a condition for theft. Moreover, the shroud after burial is considered of low value. The Hanafis support this with the athar of Imam al-Zuhri from the time of Mu'awiyah, where the Companions did not amputate. The opposing view is held by Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf (from the Hanafi school itself), who argue that the grave robber is a thief and should be cut, citing a narration from al-Sha'bi. However, the Hanafi position prevails based on the principle of hirz. As for the plunderer and swindler, the hadith of Jabir explicitly exempts them, because their taking is open, not secret. Secret taking (ikhfa') is an essential element of sarqah (theft). The passage explains that the plunderer takes by force and the swindler by trickery, both openly, so they lack the required secrecy.

Key Terms
النباش (al-nabbash): One who steals shrouds from graves
المنتهب (al-muntahib): One who takes property openly by force or plunder
المختلس (al-mukhtalis): One who takes property by trickery or swindle
الحِرْز (al-hirz): A secure place, a condition for the application of hadd amputation for theft.
الإخفاء (al-ikhfa'): Secrecy in taking, a necessary element of theft (sarqah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the hand of a grave robber (al-nabbash) not amputated?
A) Because the shroud belongs to the deceased, not the living.
B) Because the graveyard is not considered a secure place (hirz) and the shroud becomes lowly.
C) Because the grave robber is not considered a thief.
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade cutting the hand of a grave robber.

Key Takeaway
The hand is not cut for grave robbers, plunderers, and swindlers because they lack the essential condition of secret takings from a secure place (hirz).

Theft from Public Treasury and Joint Property Does Not Require Amputation
Ruling on theft from public treasury and joint property – no amputation due to shared ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal basis for why theft from bayt al-mal or joint property does not incur hand amputation, and apply the shubha (doubt) principle to new theft scenarios.

Core Idea
A thief's share in the stolen property creates a doubt (shubha) that suspends the hadd of amputation.

This passage addresses a specific exception to the general rule of hand amputation for theft. The Hanafi school holds that if the thief has any ownership share in the stolen property—whether through being a Muslim with a right to the public treasury or through joint ownership—the hadd is not applied. The ruling is based on clear prophetic example and companion practice, establishing a principle that any share of ownership creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core ruling: no amputation for theft from bayt al-mal or property where the thief has a share.
The matn states this explicitly.
2
Understand the Principle
Recognize the underlying principle: any ownership share of the thief in the stolen property creates a doubt (shubha) that blocks the hadd.
The commentary explains: because the thief has a share, the property is not completely another's.
3
Examine the Hadith Proof
Analyze the hadith of the slave of khums: the Prophet did not amputate and said 'مال الله سرق بعضه بعضا' (Allah's property, part stole from part).
The Prophet's statement indicates shared ownership of the khums among Muslims.
4
Examine the Companion Athar
Review the reports from Ali and Umar: both confirmed no amputation for theft from bayt al-mal, as the thief has a share.
These athar reinforce the principle with practical application.
5
Apply to Joint Property
Extend the same ruling to any joint property where the thief co-owns: the share creates a shubha, so no amputation.
The rule is not limited to public funds; it applies to all co-owned property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُقطع السارق من بيت المال ولا من مال للسارق فيه شركة

(۱۷) بیت المال سے چرانے والے کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا اور نہ اس مال میں جس میں چور کی شرکت ہو۔ کوئی آدمی بیت المال سے چوری کرے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یا چور کا بھی مال تھا اور دوسرے کا بھی مال ساتھ میں تھا اس میں سے چور نے چوری کر لی تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ کسی مال میں چور کا کچھ بھی حصہ ہو تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور مومن ہونے کی حیثیت سے بیت المال کے مال میں کچھ نہ کچھ چور کا بھی حصہ ہے اس لئے اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اسی طرح شرکت کے مال میں چور کا حصہ ہے اس لئے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ان عبدا من رقيق الخمس سرق من الخمس فرفع ذلک الی النبی صلى الله عليه وسلم فلم يقطعه , وقال مال الله عز وجل سرق بعضه بعضا (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب العبد يسرق ص ۳۷۲ نمبر ۲۵۹۰) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ اس کا حصہ تھا اس لئے چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا گیا (۲) عن على انه كان يقول ليس على من سرق من بيت المال قطع (ب) (سن للبیہقی ، باب من سرق من بیت المال هیاج ثامن ص ۲۸۹ نمبر ۷۳۰۴ ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۸۰ فی الرجل يسرق من بیت المال ما علیه؟ ج خامس ص ۵۱۳ نمبر ۲۸۵۵۴ (۳) اثر میں یہ بھی ہے۔ عن القاسم ان رجلا سرق من بيت المال فكتب فيه سعد الى عمر فكتب عمر الى سعد ليس عليه قطع له فيه نصيب (ج) (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ٨٠ في الرجل يسرق من بيت المال ما علیه؟ ج خامس ص ۵۱۳ نمبر ۱۲۸۵۵۴ مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل يسرق شیالہ فیہ نصیب ج عاشر ص ۲۱۲ نمبر۱۸۸۷۲) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کسی کی شرکت ہو تو اس کے چرانے سے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
And the thief is not cut from the public treasury nor from property in which the thief has a share. (17) The hand of one who steals from the public treasury is not cut, nor from property in which the thief has a share. If a person steals from bayt al-mal, his hand is not cut. Or if there was property belonging to the thief and also belonging to another, and the thief steals from it, his hand is not cut. This issue is based on the principle that if the thief has any share in the property, the hand is not cut. And because the thief, being a Muslim, has a share in the public treasury, so his hand is not cut. Similarly, in joint property, the thief has a share, so his hand is not cut. (2) In the hadith: from Ibn Abbas: 'A slave from the khums captives stole from the khums, and that was brought to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he did not cut his hand, and said: "The property of Allah, part of it has stolen from another part."' (Ibn Majah) From this hadith, it is understood that because he had a share, his hand was not cut. (2) From Ali: 'He used to say: There is no cutting for one who steals from the public treasury.' (al-Bayhaqi, Ibn Abi Shaybah) (3) Also in the athar: from al-Qasim: 'A man stole from the public treasury, so Sa'd wrote to Umar, and Umar wrote to Sa'd: There is no cutting upon him, because he has a share in it.' (Ibn Abi Shaybah, Abd al-Razzaq) From this athar, it is understood that if the thief has a share, his hand is not cut.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the matn that the thief who steals from the public treasury (bayt al-mal) or from property in which he has a share is not subject to hand amputation. The commentary explains the reasoning: the thief's own share in the property creates a doubt (shubha) that prevents the application of the hadd penalty. This is based on the principle that any share of ownership in the stolen property removes the amputation. The proofs include the hadith of the slave of khums where the Prophet did not amputate, and the athar of Ali and the correspondence between Sa'd and Umar. All indicate that when the thief has a right in the property, the hadd is suspended.

Key Terms
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslims
شركة (shirka): Joint ownership or partnership

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the public treasury belongs to the state, not the individual.
D) Because the thief must be a non-Muslim for amputation.

Key Takeaway
A thief's own share in the stolen property creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the application of the hadd of amputation, as proven by prophetic example and Companion practice.

Theft Between Close Relatives: The Two Principles Preventing Hadd
Ruling with proof: theft among relatives and master-slave does not incur hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi principles that prevent hand amputation in theft among close relatives and apply them to determine when the hadd is dropped.

Core Idea
The hand is not cut when close access or presumed share creates doubt (shubha) in theft.

We often think theft always deserves the hadd penalty, but Islamic law recognizes that family bonds change the nature of property access. This lesson explores a case where the hand is not amputated: theft between parents and children, spouses, master and slave. The Hanafi scholars ground this on two principles: lack of a secure place (hirz) and a presumed share in the property, each creating a shubha that prevents the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempted relationships
List all categories mentioned: parents, children, mahram relatives, spouses, and master-slave. Note that the text explicitly includes theft from a master's wife and from a mukatib slave.
The ruling covers both ascending and descending lineages and all mahram relatives.
2
Understand the first principle: lack of hirz
The first principle states that close relatives have such mutual access that one's home is not a secure place (hirz) for the other. For example, a father's house is not hirz for his son. Without hirz, the condition for theft is missing.
Hirz is a condition for sariqa; without it, the act is not considered theft for hadd purposes.
3
Understand the second principle: presumed share
Each party considers that they have a share or permission in the other's property. The son thinks he has a right in his father's wealth; the father considers his son's property permissible. This creates a shubha (doubt) akin to ownership, which prevents the hadd.
The text references an earlier athar or hadith that links having a share to dropping the cut.
4
See how the passage extends by qiyas
Using the father-son case as the original (asl), the ruling is analogized to all mahram relatives, spouses, and master-slave relationships on the basis of the same 'illa (mutual access or presumed share).
The text explicitly says 'the remaining cases can be analogized'.
5
Confirm the practical effect
The mufta bihi position is that amputation is not applied in any of these relationships. This is consistent across the Hanafi school and agreed upon by all madhahib.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded for this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من سرق من ابويه او ولده او ذی رحم محرم منه لم يقطع وكذلك اذا سرق احد الزوجين من الآخر او العبد من سيده

(۱۸) کسی نے چرائی اپنے والدین کی کوئی چیز ، یا اپنے بیٹے کی بازی رحم محرم کی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ ایسے ہی اگر چرائی بیوی شوہر میں سے ایک نے دوسرے کی ، یا غلام نے آقا کی یا آقا کی بیوی کی یا سیدہ کے شوہر کی یا آقا اپنے مکاتب کی چیز ۔ کسی نے ماں باپ کی چیز دس درہم سے اوپر کی چمرالی یا ماں باپ نے بیٹے کی چیز چرالی یا اپنے ذی رحم محرم کی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یا بیوی نے شوہر کی چیز چرالی یا شوہر نے بیوی کی چیز چرالی یا غلام نے آقا کی چیز چرالی یا آقا کی بیوی کی چیز چرالی تب بھی ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یا غلام نے اپنے سیدہ جو عورت تھی اس کی چیز چرائی یا سیدہ کے شوہر کی چیز چرائی یا آقا نے اپنے مکاتب کی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہ مسئلے دو اصولوں پر متفرع ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ یہ لوگ ایک دوسرے کے اتنے قریب رہتے ہیں کہ اس کے لئے اس کا گھر محفوظ نہیں رہا ، مثلا باپ کے لئے بیٹے کا اور بیٹے کے لئے باپ کا گھر محفوظ اور حرز نہیں ہے۔ اس پر باقی مسئلے قیاس کر لیں۔ اور چوری کہتے ہیں مقام محفوظ سے چپکے سے اٹھانا۔ اس لئے چوری نہیں پائی گئی اس لئے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور دوسرا اصول یہ ہے کہ ایک کا دوسرے کے مال میں کچھ نہ کچھ حصہ سمجھا جاتا ہے۔ مثلا بیٹا سمجھتا ہے کہ باپ کے مال میں میرا حصہ ہے اور باپ بھی سمجھتا ہے کہ بیٹے کا مال میرے لئے مباح ہے۔ اور اوپر اثر اور حدیث گزری کہ مال میں کچھ نہ کچھ حصہ ہو تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہی حال غلام اور آقا کے درمیان کا ہے۔ اور یہی حال بیوی اور شوہر کے
And whoever steals from his parents, or his child, or a mahram relative, his hand is not cut; and likewise when one of the spouses steals from the other, or the slave from his master. (18) If someone stole something from his parents, or his son's mahram relative, then his hand is not cut. Similarly, if one of the spouses stole from the other, or the slave from his master, or the master from his wife, or the mistress's husband, or the master from his kitaba slave. ... These cases are derived from two principles: One is that these people are so close to each other that for one, the other's house is not secure. For example, for a father, his son's house is not secure and not a hirz. The remaining cases can be analogized. And theft is defined as taking secretly from a secure place. So here theft is not found, so the hand is not cut. The second principle is that each is considered to have some share in the other's property. For example, the son considers that he has a share in his father's wealth, and the father also considers that his son's property is permissible for him. And the previous athar and hadith established that if the thief has any share in the property, the hand is not cut. This is also the case between slave and master, and between wife and husband.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that theft between close relatives does not result in hand amputation. The Arabic matn lists: parents, child, mahram relative, spouses, and master-slave. The commentary explains the reasoning based on two principles: (1) Lack of hirz: the close relationship means that these people have mutual access to each other's homes, so the stolen property is not taken from a secure place. For example, a father has open access to his son's house, thus the son's house is not a hirz for the father. (2) Presumed share: each party considers the other's property as having some claim or permission. This creates a doubt (shubha) similar to having a share, which prevents hadd. The commentary links back to the earlier proofs about sharing in property.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., mother, sister, aunt)
حرز (hirz): A secure place for property
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents application of hadd penalties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a son not amputated for stealing from his father's house?
A) Because the father's property is not valuable.
B) Because the son has access to his father's home, so it is not a secure place (hirz) for him.
C) Because the son must inherit the property anyway.
D) Because the father must forgive the son.

Key Takeaway
The hand is not cut in theft between close relatives because either the property was not taken from a secure place (hirz) or the thief has a presumed share creating a shubha.
