Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
10 min
Exceptions to Hadd for Theft: Share and Relationship Principles
Explains when the hadd punishment for theft is not applied due to shared ownership or close relationships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi principles that exempt certain thefts from hadd and apply them to the specific relationships listed in the text.

Core Idea
Hadd for theft is waived when the thief has a share in the property or the relationship destroys the security of hirz.

This passage from Kitab al-Sariqa outlines several cases where the hand of a thief is not cut despite meeting the apparent conditions of theft. The Hanafi school derives these exemptions from two fundamental principles: the lack of a secure place (hirz) due to close relationships, and the presumed share in ownership between certain parties. Understanding these exceptions is crucial to properly applying the hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal principles
The text explicitly states two principles: (1) close relationship makes the home not a secure place (hirz) for the other, so theft is not fully established; (2) each party is considered to have some share in the other's property, negating complete ownership.
These principles are general and apply qiyas to other similar cases.
2
List the relationships exempted
Parents and children, mahram relatives, spouses, slave and master, master and mukatib, and theft from Bayt al-Mal or ghanimah.
The list is exhaustive for this passage; other relationships may be analogized.
3
Analyze the primary dalil
The hadith of Ibn Abbas regarding the slave of khums stealing from khums; the Prophet's statement 'Allah's wealth, some of it stole some of it' indicates the share principle.
This hadith is the foundational evidence for the share exception.
4
Examine supplementary athar
The passage cites athar from Ali, Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, and others that reinforce the same rulings for Bayt al-Mal, slave-master, and spouse theft.
These strengthen the Hanafi position and show its continuity with early practice.
5
Apply the principles to a new case
For example, theft from war booty (ghanimah) is also exempted based on the share principle, as stated at the end of the passage.
This demonstrates the generalizability of the two principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
بيت المال ولا من مال للسارق فيه شركة ٢٥٠ (١) و من سرق من ابويه او ولده او ذی رحم محرم منه لم يقطع وكذلك اذا سرق احد الزوجين من الآخر او العبد من سيده
میں کچھ نہ کچھ چور کا بھی حصہ ہے اس لئے اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اسی طرح شرکت کے مال میں چور کا حصہ ہے اس لئے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا
الله
جائے گا (٢) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ان عبدا من رقيق الخمس سرق من الخمس فرفع ذلک الی النبی الله
فلم يقطعه ، وقال مال الله عز وجل سرق بعضه بعضا (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب العبد يسرق ص ٣٧٢ نمبر ٢٥٩٠) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ اس کا حصہ تھا اس لئے چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا گیا (٢) عن على انه كان يقول ليس على من سرق من بيت المال قطع (ب) (سن للبیہقی ، باب من سرق من بیت المال هیاج ثامن ص ٢٨٩ نمبر ٧٣٠٤ ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ٨٠ فی الرجل يسرق من بیت المال ما علیه؟ ج خامس ص ٥١٣ نمبر ٢٨٥٥٤ (٣) اثر میں یہ بھی ہے۔ عن القاسم ان رجلا سرق من بيت المال فكتب فيه سعد الى عمر فكتب عمر الى سعد ليس عليه قطع له فيه نصيب (ج) (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ٨٠ في الرجل يسرق من بيت المال ما علیه؟ ج خامس ص ٥١٣ نمبر ١٢٨٥٥٤ مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل يسرق شیالہ فیہ نصیب ج عاشر ص ٢١٢ نمبر١٨٨٧٢) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کسی کی شرکت ہو تو اس کے چرانے سے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
٢٥٤٠] (١٨) کسی نے چرائی اپنے والدین کی کوئی چیز ، یا اپنے بیٹے کی بازی رحم محرم کی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ ایسے ہی اگر چرائی بیوی شوہر میں سے ایک نے دوسرے کی ، یا غلام نے آقا کی یا آقا کی بیوی کی یا سیدہ کے شوہر کی یا آقا اپنے مکاتب کی چیز ۔ کسی نے ماں باپ کی چیز دس درہم سے اوپر کی چمرالی یا ماں باپ نے بیٹے کی چیز چرالی یا اپنے ذی رحم محرم کی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یا بیوی نے شوہر کی چیز چرالی یا شوہر نے بیوی کی چیز چرالی یا غلام نے آقا کی چیز چرالی یا آقا کی بیوی کی چیز چرالی تب بھی ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یا غلام نے اپنے سیدہ جو عورت تھی اس کی چیز چرائی یا سیدہ کے شوہر کی چیز چرائی یا آقا نے اپنے مکاتب کی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں
کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہ مسئلے دو اصولوں پر متفرع ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ یہ لوگ ایک دوسرے کے اتنے قریب رہتے ہیں کہ اس کے لئے اس کا گھر محفوظ نہیں رہا ، مثلا باپ کے لئے بیٹے کا اور بیٹے کے لئے باپ کا گھر محفوظ اور حرز نہیں ہے۔ اس پر باقی مسئلے قیاس کر لیں۔ اور چوری کہتے ہیں مقام محفوظ سے چپکے سے اٹھانا۔ اس لئے چوری نہیں پائی گئی اس لئے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اور دوسرا اصول یہ ہے کہ ایک کا دوسرے کے مال میں کچھ نہ کچھ حصہ سمجھا جاتا ہے۔ مثلا بیٹا سمجھتا ہے کہ باپ کے مال میں میرا حصہ ہے اور باپ بھی سمجھتا ہے کہ بیٹے کا مال میرے لئے مباح ہے۔ اور اوپر اثر اور حدیث گزری کہ مال میں کچھ نہ کچھ حصہ ہو تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ یہی حال غلام اور آقا کے درمیان کا ہے۔ اور یہی حال بیوی اور شوہر کے
حاشیه : (الف) حضرت ابن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ شمس کے غلام نے نفس کا مال چھایا۔ پس اس کو حضور کے پاس لے گئے تو آنے اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا۔ اور فرمایا اللہ کا مال ہے بعض نے بعض کو چرا ایا (ب) حضرت علی فرمایا کرتے تھے جس نے بیت المال سے چرایا اس پر ہاتھ کا ٹا نہیں ہے (ج) حضرت قاسم سے منقول ہے کہ ایک
آدمی نے بیت المال سے چرایا تو حضرت سعد نے حضرت عمر کولکھا تو حضرت عمر نے حضرت سعد کو لکھا کہ اس پر ہاتھ کا شانہیں ہے کیونکہ اس میں چور کا حصہ ہے۔

او من امراة سيده او من زوج سيدته او المولى من مكاتبه [ ٢٥٤١] (١٩) وکذلک
درمیان کا ہے۔ اس لئے غلام آقا کی چرالے یا آقا غلام مکاتب کی چرالے، بیوی شوہر کی چرالے یا شوہر بیوی کی چھالے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (٢) اثر میں ہے۔سال ابن مسعود فقال عبدی سرق قباء عبدی قال مالک سرق بعضه بعضا لا قطع فيه وهو قول ابن عباس (الف) ( سنن بیہقی ، باب العبد يسرق من متاع سیده جمعنا من ص ٤٨٨ نمبر ٧٣٠٢ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ٨١ فی العبد يسرق من ابی شبیبته مولاه ما علیه؟ ج خامس ص ٥١٤ نمبر ٢٨٥٥٩) (٢) اثر میں ہے۔ فقال له عمر ماذا سرق قال سرق مرآة لامرأتي ثمنها ستون درهما فقال ارسله فلیس علیه قطع خادکم سرق متاعكم (ب) ( سنن بیہقی ، باب العبد يسرق من مال امرأة سيده ج ثامن ص ٤٨٩ نمبر ١٧٣٠٣) ان آثار سے معلوم ہوا کہ غلام آقا کے مال کو چرائے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
بیوی شوہر کے بارے میں یہ اثر ہے۔ بلغنی عن عامر قال ليس على زوج المرأة في سرقة متاعها قطع ۔ اور دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ وقال عبد الكريم ليس على المرأة في سرقة مناعه قطع (ج) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب من سرق مالا مقطع فيدج عاشرص ٢٢١ نمبر ١٨٩٠٨) اور اسی باب کے حدیث نمبر ١٨٩٠٧ میں ذی رحم محرم کے بارے میں ہے۔ قال الثوري ويستحسن الا يقطع من سرق من ذي رحم محرم خاله او عمه او ذات محرم (د) (مصنف عبد الرزاق ج عاشر نمبر ١٨٩٠٧) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ بیوی شوہر کا یا شوہر بیوی کا مال چہائے یا ذی رحم محرم آدمی چرائے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
مکاتب کا مال آقا کا مال ہے اس لئے آقا مکاتب کا مال چرائے تو نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ ان سارے مسئلوں کے لئے اوپر کی حدیث ابن ماجہ شریف دلیل ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ان عبـدا مــن رقيق الخمس سرق من الخمس فرفع ذلك الى النبي الا الا الله فلم يقطعه وقال مال الله عز وجل سرق بعضه بعضا (ه) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب العبد یسر ق ص ٢٧٢ نمبر ٢٥٩٠) اس حدیث کے آخر میں ہے بعض بعض کا چرایا اس لئے نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (٢) یہ اثر بھی ہے۔ عن الثورى قال ان سـرق الــمـكـاتــب من سيده شيئا لم يقطع وان سرق السيد من المكاتب شيئا لم يقطع (و)(مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الخيانة ج عاشر ص ٢١١ نمبر ١٨٨٧)
(٢٥٤١) (١٩) ایسے ہی مال غنیمت میں چرائے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت عبد اللہ ابن مسعودؓ سے پوچھا میرے غلام نے میرے دوسرے غلام کی قباء چرالی۔ فرمایا تمہارا ہی مال ہے بعض نے بعض کا چرایا۔ اس لئے اس پر ہاتھ کاٹنا نہیں ہے۔ اور یہی قول حضرت عبد اللہ ابن عباس کا ہے (ب) حضرت عمرؓ نے پوچھا کیا چرایا؟ کہا میری بیوی کا آئینہ چرایا جس کی قیمت ساٹھ درہم تھی۔ فرمایا اس کو چھوڑ دو اس پر ہاتھ کا نا نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تمہارے خام نے تمہارا سامان چرایا ہے ( ج ) حضرت عامر سے منقول ہے کہ عورت کے شوہر پر عورت کا سامان چرانے میں کا شنا نہیں ہے، دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ حضرت عبد الکریم نے فرمایا عورت پر شوہر کے سامنے چرانے میں ہاتھ کا نا نہیں ہے (د) حضرت ثوری نے اچھا سمجھا کہ نہ کاٹے جس نے چرایا ذی رحم محرم کے مال سے مثلا ماموں یا چچا بازی رحم محرم (ہ) حضرت ابن عباس سے منقول ہے کہ شمس کے غلام نے خمس سے مال چہ ایا۔ پس یہ معاملہ حضور کے پاس لے گئے تو ہاتھ نہیںکا ٹا اور فرمایا اللہ کا مال ہے بعض نے بعض کو چرایا ( و ) حضرت ثوری نے فرمایا اگر مکاتب نے آقا کی کوئی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کا ٹا جائے گا۔ اور اگر آقا نے مکاتب کی کوئی چیز چھالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔
Book of Theft: Bayt al-Mal and [any] wealth in which the thief has a share [is exempt]. And whoever steals from his parents or his child or a mahram relative, his hand is not cut. Similarly, if one spouse steals from the other, or a slave from his master. (Urdu commentary: In it the thief also has some share, therefore his hand will not be cut. Similarly, in partnership property the thief has a share, so the hand is not cut.) (2) There is a hadith: From Ibn Abbas: A slave from the khums slaves stole from the khums, and this was brought to the Prophet ﷺ, and he did not cut his hand and said: 'The wealth of Allah, some of it has stolen some of it.' (Ibn Majah, Book of the Slave Stealing, p. 372, no. 2590) In this hadith, because he had a share, the thief's hand was not cut. (2) From Ali: He used to say: 'There is no cutting for one who steals from Bayt al-Mal.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter: Whoever steals from Bayt al-Mal, vol. 8, p. 289, no. 7304; and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, vol. 5, p. 513, no. 28554) (3) There is also an athar: From al-Qasim: A man stole from Bayt al-Mal, so Sa'd wrote to Umar about it, and Umar wrote to Sa'd: 'There is no cutting on him because he has a share in it.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, vol. 5, p. 513, no. 128554; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, vol. 10, p. 212, no. 18872) From this athar it is understood that if there is partnership, then one stealing from it, the hand is not cut. [2540] (18) If someone steals something from his parents, or from his child, or from a mahram relative, then the hand will not be cut. Similarly, if one spouse steals from the other, or a slave from his master, or from the master's wife, or from the mistress's husband, or the master from his mukatib. If someone steals from parents something worth more than ten dirhams, or parents steal from their child, or steal from a mahram relative, the hand is not cut. Or wife from husband, husband from wife, slave from master, or from master's wife, then also the hand is not cut. Or a slave steals from his female master, or from her husband, or the master steals from his mukatib, then the hand will not be cut. These issues are derived from two principles: First, these people live so close to each other that for one, the other's home is not secure; e.g., a father's home is not secure for a son, and a son's home is not secure for a father. Apply qiyas for the rest. And theft is defined as taking stealthily from a secure place. Therefore, theft is not established, so the hand is not cut. Second principle: One is considered to have some share in the other's property. For example, a son thinks he has a share in his father's property, and a father thinks his son's wealth is permissible for him. And the previous athar and hadith passed that if there is some share in the property, the hand is not cut. The same situation applies between slave and master, and between wife and husband. Footnote: (a) Ibn Abbas said: The slave of Khums stole the property of Khums, so it was brought to the Prophet, and he did not cut his hand and said: 'Allah's wealth, some of it has stolen some of it.' (b) Ali used to say: 'There is no cutting on one who steals from Bayt al-Mal.' (c) Narrated from Qasim: A man stole from Bayt al-Mal, so Sa'd wrote to Umar, and Umar wrote that there is no cutting because the thief has a share in it. [2541] (19) Similarly, [if he steals] from the master's wife, or from the husband of his mistress, or the master from his mukatib. (Urdu commentary: The same situation applies. Therefore, if a slave steals from his master, or a master from his slave mukatib, or wife from husband, or husband from wife, the hand will not be cut. (2) There is an athar: Ibn Mas'ud was asked: 'My slave stole the qaba of my [another] slave.' He said: 'It is your property; some of it stole some of it; there is no cutting.' And this is the saying of Ibn Abbas. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter: The slave steals from his master's goods, vol. 8, p. 488, no. 7302; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, vol. 5, p. 514, no. 28559) (2) Another athar: Umar asked: 'What did he steal?' He said: 'He stole a mirror of my wife worth sixty dirhams.' He said: 'Release him; there is no cutting on him because your servant stole your goods.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter: The slave steals from his master's wife's property, vol. 8, p. 489, no. 17303) From these athar, it is understood that if a slave steals from his master's property, the hand is not cut. Regarding husband and wife, there is this athar: It reached me from Amir: 'There is no cutting on a woman's husband for stealing her goods.' And in another narration: Abd al-Karim said: 'There is no cutting on a woman for stealing her husband's goods.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter: Whoever steals property on which there is no cutting, vol. 10, p. 221, no. 18908) And in the same chapter, hadith no. 18907 concerning a mahram relative: al-Thawri said: 'It is considered good (yustahsan) not to cut one who steals from a mahram relative: his maternal uncle, paternal uncle, or other mahram.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, vol. 10, no. 18907) From this athar, it is understood that if a wife steals from her husband or a husband from his wife, or a mahram relative steals, the hand will not be cut. The property of a mukatib is the property of the master, so if the master steals from the mukatib, it will not be cut. For all these issues, the above hadith of Ibn Majah is evidence: From Ibn Abbas: A slave from the khums slaves stole from the khums, and this was brought to the Prophet, and he did not cut his hand and said: 'Allah's wealth, some of it has stolen some of it.' (Ibn Majah, Book of the Slave Stealing, p. 272, no. 2590) At the end of this hadith, it says: 'some of it stole some of it,' so it is not cut. (2) There is also this athar: From al-Thawri: 'If a mukatib steals from his master something, it is not cut; if the master steals from the mukatib something, it is not cut.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter: Betrayal, vol. 10, p. 211, no. 1887) [2541] (19) Similarly, if he steals from the war booty, the hand is not cut. Footnotes: (a) Abdullah ibn Mas'ud was asked: 'My slave stole the qaba of my [other] slave.' He said: 'It is your property; some of it stole some of it; there is no cutting for it.' And this is the saying of Abdullah ibn Abbas. (b) Umar asked: 'What did he steal?' He said: 'A mirror of my wife worth sixty dirhams.' He said: 'Release him; there is no cutting on him because your servant stole your goods.' (c) From Amir: 'There is no cutting on a woman's husband for stealing her goods.' In another narration: Abd al-Karim said: 'There is no cutting on a woman for stealing her husband's goods.' (d) Al-Thawri considered it good not to cut one who steals from a mahram relative, e.g., maternal uncle, paternal uncle, or mahram. (e) From Ibn Abbas: The slave of Khums stole from the khums, so this was taken to the Prophet, and he did not cut his hand and said: 'Allah's wealth, some of it has stolen some of it.' (f) Al-Thawri said: 'If a mukatib steals from his master something, his hand is not cut; if the master steals from the mukatib something, his hand is not cut.'
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Kitab al-Sariqa, focusing on exceptions to the hadd penalty of amputation. The author lists situations where the thief's hand is not cut despite the act of taking property. The text first addresses theft from Bayt al-Mal (public treasury) where the thief has a share, citing the hadith of Ibn Abbas where the Prophet ﷺ did not cut the hand of a slave who stole from the khums, stating that it is Allah's wealth and some of it stole some of it. Then it extends this principle to theft from parents, children, mahram relatives, spouses, slaves from masters, etc. The author provides two underlying principles: (1) The close relationship makes the home not a secure place (hirz) for the other, so theft is not fully established; (2) Each is considered to have a share in the other's property, negating the complete ownership required for hadd. The text then brings additional athar from Ibn Mas'ud, Umar, and others to support the rulings. Finally, it states that the same applies to war booty. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each case and the evidence. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh, as these rulings are standard in the school.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept; theft from a non-hirz does not constitute sariqa subject to hadd.
سرقة (sariqa): The act of taking property stealthily from a secure place (hirz) with a minimum threshold (nisab).
مکاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, paying in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a scenario where the hadd is waived?
A) A father stealing from his son.
B) A son stealing from his father.
C) A stranger stealing from a shop.
D) A slave stealing from his master.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school waives the hadd for theft when the thief has a share in the property or stands in a close relationship that negates the security of hirz.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The thief's share in the spoils prevents cutting the hand.

This short passage presents a key exception to the general rule of the hadd of theft. In Hanafi fiqh, stealing from the spoils of war (ghanimah) does not trigger amputation, even if the thief is not entitled to the specific item. The reasoning hinges on the thief's ownership interest in the spoils, which creates doubt that bars the fixed punishment.

Guided Path

3
Trace the rationale
The thief has a share in the spoils, so the property is not entirely another's, creating shubha (doubt) that repels the hadd.
Principle: hudud are repelled by doubts.

Definition of Hiraz: Two Types and Their Legal Effect
Defines hiraz and gives ruling with prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of hiraz in Hanafi theft law and apply the condition that makes cutting wajib.

Core Idea
Hiraz is of two types: inherent security and guardian-presence; theft from either necessitates cutting.

The concept of hiraz (secure place) is central to the Hanafi law of theft (sariqa). This passage from Sharh al-Sameri clarifies that hiraz is not limited to locked buildings; it also includes any place where the owner is personally guarding the property. The ruling is supported by two Prophetic hadith that demonstrate both types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of hiraz
Read the opening line: 'Wal-hirzu ala darbayn' – hiraz is of two types. Understand that hiraz is a place that prevents easy access to property.
Hiraz is a condition for the hadd punishment of cutting the hand.
2
Distinguish the two types
First type: inherent hiraz (like houses and rooms) – secure by their nature. Second type: hiraz by a guardian – any location becomes secure if the owner is present and guarding.
The guardian's presence makes even an open space equivalent to a hiraz.
3
Note the condition for cutting
The text says: 'man saraqa shay'an min hirzin aw ghayri hirzin wa sahibuhu 'indahu yahfazuhu wajaba 'alayhi al-qat'. So theft from either type of hiraz OR from a non-hiraz with the owner guarding triggers cutting.
The presence of the owner is crucial for the second type.
4
Analyze the evidence (dalil)
Two hadith are cited. The first (Ibn Umar) proves inherent hiraz: a shield stolen from a women's room (suffat al-nisa') – that room is inherently secure. The second (Safwan) proves hiraz by guardian: Safwan was sleeping in the mosque (not inherently secure) but his presence as guardian made it a hiraz.
These hadith show that the Prophet applied cutting in both situations.
5
Apply the ruling
In any theft case, first determine if the property was in an inherent hiraz. If not, check if the owner was present guarding it. If neither, the hadd is not applied.
Other conditions (nisab, secrecy, etc.) are not discussed here but are also required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحرز على ضربين: حرز لمعنى فيه كالدور والبيوت، وحرز بالحافظ. فمن سرق شيئا من حرز أو غير حرز وصاحبه عنده يحفظه وجب عليه القطع. عن عبد الله بن عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قطع يد رجل سرق ترسا من صفة النساء ثمنه ثلاثة دراهم. وعن صفوان بن أمية قال: كنت نائما في المسجد على خميصة لي ثمن ثلاثين درهما فجاء رجل فاختلسها مني فأخذ الرجل فأتي به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فأمر به ليقطع.
And the secure place (hiraz) is of two types: a hiraz by its own meaning, like houses and rooms, and a hiraz by a guardian. So whoever steals something from a hiraz or from a non-hiraz while its owner (the owner of the property) is with him guarding it, cutting (of the hand) is obligatory upon him. On the authority of Abdullah ibn Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) cut the hand of a man who stole a shield from a women's chamber worth three dirhams. And on the authority of Safwan ibn Umayyah: I was sleeping in the mosque on my black cloak worth thirty dirhams, and a man came and snatched it from me, so the man was caught and brought to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he ordered that his hand be cut.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of hiraz (secure place) in Hanafi theft law. Hiraz is divided into two types: first, a place that is inherently secure due to its construction, such as houses and rooms; second, a place that is not inherently secure but becomes so due to the presence of a guarding person. The ruling is that if someone steals from either type of hiraz, the hand is cut. Furthermore, if the owner is present and guarding the property, even if it is not in a hiraz, the hand is cut. This is supported by two hadith: the first from Abdullah ibn Umar about a man who stole a shield from a women's room (صفة النساء), proving that a room is an inherent hiraz; the second from Safwan ibn Umayyah about his cloak being snatched while he was sleeping in the mosque, proving that the owner's presence as a guardian makes the place a hiraz, even if the mosque is generally not a hiraz because it is open to all.

Key Terms
حرز (hiraz): A secure place that prevents easy theft; either inherently secure (like a house) or made secure by a guardian.
حافظ (hafiz): A guardian or keeper who protects the property.
صفة النساء (suffat al-nisa'): The women's chamber or apartment; a specific area in a house that is inherently secure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The owner's presence guarding it.
B) The property's high value.
C) The thief's use of a tool.
D) The time being night.

Key Takeaway
Hiraz for hadd includes both inherently secure places and places where the owner guards the property.

Theft from a Public Bathhouse: No Cutting of the Hand
States that hand is not cut for theft from hammam or public-access houses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a hammam and explain why the hand is not cut, citing the proof from the athar of Abu al-Darda'.

Core Idea
The hand is not cut for theft from a public bathhouse because it is not a secure place (hiraz).

This lesson examines a specific ruling within Islamic criminal law (hudud): theft from places that are open to the public. The Hanafi school holds that the hadd punishment of cutting the hand is not applicable when the theft occurs in a bathhouse (hammam) or any house that people are permitted to enter freely. The key legal concept is 'hiraz' (secure place). A hammam is not considered hiraz because anyone can enter, so the strict requirements for the hadd penalty are not met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act being discussed? Theft from a hammam or a public-access house. What is the ruling? The hand is not cut.
The ruling is a negative one: cutting is not wajib.
2
Understand the concept of hiraz
Hiraz is a secure place where access is restricted. The Hanafi school defines hiraz as a place that is locked or guarded, preventing free entry.
A hammam allows free entry, so it is not hiraz.
3
Examine the proof
The passage cites two proofs: the athar of Abu al-Darda' stating 'there is no cutting for the thief of the bathhouse,' and a hadith about the granary that distinguishes between theft from a secure place (jarin) and less secure places.
The hadith implies that theft from a secure place triggers the hadd; the hammam is analogous to the non-secure category.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may consider a hammam with lockers as hiraz. The Hanafi view is based on the general unrestricted access.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the rule to other public places
Any place where the public is freely admitted (e.g., libraries, parks, open marketplaces) is not hiraz for the purposes of the hadd.
The ruling is specific to the act of theft; civil liability for compensation still applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قطع على من سرق من حمام او من بيت أذن للناس في دخوله. عن أبي الدرداء قال: ليس على سارق الحمام قطع. وروي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: ومن سرق منه شيئا بعد أن يؤويه الجرين فبلغ ثمن المجن فعليه القطع، ومن سرق دون ذلك فعليه غرامة مثليه والعقوبة.
And there is no cutting (of the hand) for the one who steals from a bathhouse (hammam) or from a house that allows people to enter. On the authority of Abu al-Darda' he said: There is no cutting for the thief of the bathhouse. And it was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him): And whoever steals something from it after the granary (jarin) has received it and it reaches the price of a shield (mujann), then cutting is upon him; and whoever steals less than that, then upon him is double the penalty and punishment.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage rules that the hand is not cut for stealing from a hammam (bathhouse) or from any house that is open to the public. The reason is that such places are not considered hiraz (secure) because anyone can enter. The proof is the athar of Abu al-Darda' that states there is no cutting for the thief of the hammam. The passage also cites a hadith that indicates that for theft from a granary (jarin) after the crop has been stored, if the value reaches the price of a shield (three dirhams approximately), then the hand is cut; otherwise, only double compensation and punishment are due. This distinction shows that only theft from a hiraz triggers the hadd; public places like hammam are not hiraz.

Key Terms
حمام (hammam): A public bathhouse, where people enter freely.
بيت أذن للناس في دخوله (bayt udhina lilnnasi fi dukhulih): A house that is open to the public by permission, i.e., a place of general access.
حرز (hiraz): A secure or fortified place where property is stored and access is restricted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Stealing from the Mosque When the Owner Is Present
Establishes the rule via a hadith example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the owner's presence transforms a non-hiraz (like a mosque) into a hiraz for the purpose of hadd cutting, and distinguish this from cases where the owner is absent.

Core Idea
A mosque is not a hiraz, but if the owner is present and guarding his property, it becomes one — so theft triggers cutting.

The mosque is generally not considered a secure place (hiraz) because it is open to all. However, the Hanafi school recognizes an exception: if the owner is present and actively guarding his property, the mosque acquires the status of a hiraz. This lesson examines the ruling based on the well-known hadith of Safwan ibn Umayyah, where the Prophet ordered cutting for a theft that occurred in the mosque while the owner was sleeping on his cloak.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is stealing goods (متاع) from the mosque. The ruling is that cutting (قطع) is wajib (obligatory).
The text says 'قطع' — this refers to the hadd punishment for theft.
2
State the general principle regarding hiraz
Generally, for hadd cutting to apply, the theft must occur from a secure place (hiraz). A mosque, by default, is not a hiraz because it is accessible to everyone.
If the place is not hiraz, the thief's hand is not cut, even if the nisab is met.
3
Identify the exception introduced here
The exception is when the owner is present with his property. His presence effectively makes the mosque a hiraz for that property.
The owner's presence functions as a guardian (حافظ) over the property.
4
Examine the dalil (hadith of Safwan)
The Prophet ordered cutting for a theft from the mosque because Safwan was sleeping on his cloak, i.e., he was present and guarding it.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear and qat'i in dalala.
5
Derive the illa (effective cause)
The illa for cutting is the presence of a guardian. When the owner is present, the property is effectively protected, so the place is deemed a hiraz by virtue of the guardian.
This is an example of istihsan, though the text says it cannot be overridden by istihsan.
6
Consider the ikhtilaf (cross-madhhab dispute)
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) do not consider the owner's presence alone sufficient to make a non-hiraz into a hiraz. They may require physical enclosure.
The Hanafi school takes the stronger view based on the explicit hadith.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage clarifies that even though the mosque is generally not a hiraz (since it is open to all), if the owner of the property is present and guarding it, then it becomes a hiraz by virtue of the guardian. Therefore, stealing from the mosque with the owner present incurs the hadd of cutting. The proof is the well-known hadith of Safwan ibn Umayyah, who was sleeping in the mosque with his cloak under his head. When it was snatched, the thief was caught and the Prophet ordered the cutting of his hand. This shows that the owner's presence as a guardian makes the place equivalent to a hiraz, even if the place itself is not inherently secure.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway

No Amputation for Theft from Unsecured Premises
Ruling on theft from places with general public entry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of hirz for the hadd of theft and apply it to determine whether amputation applies in cases of theft from public places.

Core Idea
Amputation requires a secure place (hirz); public access voids this condition.

In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd penalty of hand amputation for theft is not applied unconditionally. One essential condition is that the stolen property must have been taken from a secure location (hirz). This lesson examines the ruling that theft from places such as bathhouses, mosques, and caravanserais—where the public has general permission to enter—does not incur the hadd, even if the value exceeds the nisab. The author supports this with an athar from Abu al-Darda and a hadith from Abu Dawud, and extends the ruling by qiyas to all similar places.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on theft from a bathhouse (hamam) or any house with general entry permission. The ruling: no amputation.
The act is sariqa (theft), but the ruling is conditional on the place's security.
2
Define the key condition: hirz
Understand that hirz (secure place) is a condition for amputation. A place open to all is not hirz. The bathhouse is an example.
The author explains that in the past, bathhouses were public, so they lacked security.
3
Locate and analyze the proofs
Examine the athar from Abu al-Darda (no cutting for bathhouse thief) and the hadith from Abu Dawud (cutting only after property is gathered in a secure place). Both support the condition of hirz.
The proofs are zanni in transmission and indication.
4
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
Note that the ruling is extended by qiyas to all places with general permission to enter, such as mosques and caravanserais. The common illa is the lack of exclusive access.
This shows the Hanafi method of extending rulings via shared effective cause.

The commentary al-Thamiri, Volume 3, page 422, Book of Theft. (2544) (22) There is no amputation on one who steals from a bathhouse or from a house that people have permission to enter. (2545) (23) One who steals goods from a mosque while the owner has them with him, amputation is applied. (2544) (24) There is no amputation on one who steals from a bathhouse or from a house where people are allowed to enter. In the past, baths were built for bathing where anyone could enter, so that place was not secure. Similarly, any place where everyone is allowed entry, like a mosque or caravanserai, those places are not secure (hirz), so there will be no amputation for stealing from them. There is an athar from Abu al-Darda: 'There is no amputation on the thief of a bathhouse.' (Sunan al-Muttaqi, etc.) From this athar, it is understood that if one steals from a bathhouse (i.e., a washing place), the hand is not cut. And by analogy, all places where everyone is allowed entry: stealing from them does not require amputation. Because in a bathhouse, everyone is allowed entry, so the hand is not cut. (2) In a hadith from Abu Dawud: 'Whoever steals something after it is gathered in the threshing floor and it reaches the price of a shield, then amputation is upon him; whoever steals less than that, then upon him is double the penalty and punishment.' From this hadith, it is understood that if the item has not reached a secure place, the hand is not cut, but rather double compensation is required.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about theft from places that are not considered secure (hirz). The Hanafi position is that amputation of the hand for theft is only applied when the stolen property is taken from a secure location (hirz). A bathhouse (hamam) or any place with general permission for entry is not hirz because it is accessible to all. The author cites an athar from Abu al-Darda that there is no cutting for the thief of a bathhouse. He extends this by qiyas (analogy) to all similar locations like mosques and caravanserais. A hadith from Abu Dawud emphasizes that the property must be secured (e.g., in a threshing floor) for amputation; otherwise, only double penalty is due. Thus, the condition of hirz is essential for the hadd penalty.

Key Terms
حمام (ḥammām): Public bathhouse where anyone can enter
حرز (ḥirz): A secure place that protects property from unauthorized taking

Theft from Mosque with Owner Present: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on theft from mosque with owner present

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for amputation in mosque theft and apply the hadith of Safwan to determine when the mosque becomes a hirz.

Core Idea
A mosque is not a hirz unless the owner is present, creating temporary security.

This lesson addresses a key distinction in Hanafi hudud law: theft from a public place like a mosque. While the mosque itself is not considered a secure location (hirz) due to open access, the owner's presence with the property transforms it into a guarded space, justifying the hadd penalty of amputation. We will examine the ruling, its proof from the hadith of Safwan, and the underlying qiyas on other public places.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
State the general rule: theft from a mosque does not incur amputation because the mosque is not a hirz. Then identify the exception: if the owner is present with the property, amputation applies.
The condition is possession of the property by the owner (ṣāḥibuhu ʿindahu).
2
Locate the dalil and its wording
Recall the hadith of Safwan ibn Umayya, which the author cites. Note that the owner was sleeping with his cloak under his head, effectively guarding it.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud and Nasai.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet ordered amputation because the cloak was under Safwan's head, indicating presence and guarding. The mosque alone did not make it secure.
The proof relies on the owner's act of guarding, not the location.
4
Apply qiyas to similar places
The text analogizes other public places (bathhouse, caravanserai) to the mosque: without a guard, no amputation. The athar from Abu al-Darda supports this.
Qiyas used: 'bathhouse is like mosque' due to general entry.
5
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Other schools may consider the mosque itself a hirz. The Hanafi position is distinct: only owner presence creates a hirz.
This affects fatwa in jurisdictions with different madhhab.

حکم دیا ہاتھ کاٹنے کا۔
(2545) (23) And one who steals goods from a mosque with the owner having them with him, amputation is applied. There is no amputation on one who steals from a bathhouse or a house with general entry permission. Similarly, any place with general entry permission like a mosque or caravanserai, those places are not secure, so no amputation for theft from them. There is an athar from Abu al-Darda: 'There is no amputation on the thief of a bathhouse.' From this it is understood that theft from a bathhouse does not necessitate hand cutting. And by analogy, all places where everyone is allowed entry: theft from them does not require cutting. Because in a bathhouse everyone can enter. (2) In a hadith from Abu Dawud: 'Whoever steals something after it is gathered in the threshing floor and it reaches the price of a shield, then amputation is upon him; whoever steals less than that, upon him is double the penalty and punishment.' (2545) (23) Someone stole goods from the mosque and its owner had them with him, then the hand is cut. In a mosque, ordinary people can go, so if goods are left unattended and stolen, the hand is not cut. But if the owner is guarding them and they are stolen, then amputation is applied. The hadith of Safwan ibn Umayya has passed: he was sleeping in the Sacred Mosque with a cloak under his head, and a man snatched it. The man was brought to the Prophet who ordered his hand to be cut. In that hadith, Safwan had the cloak under his head, so he was the guard. Therefore, amputation was due to the guard, not because of the mosque.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This lesson addresses theft in a mosque. The general rule is that a mosque is not a secure place (hirz) because people can freely enter. Therefore, if someone leaves property unattended in a mosque and it is stolen, the thief's hand is not amputated. However, if the owner is present with the property, effectively guarding it, then the property is considered secure, and amputation applies. This is supported by the hadith of Safwan ibn Umayya, who was sleeping in the mosque with his cloak under his head. The thief took it, and the Prophet ordered amputation. The key point is that the owner's presence creates a temporary secure zone. Thus, the ruling is conditional on the owner's presence.

Key Terms
صاحبه عنده (ṣāḥibuhu ʿindahu): The owner is present with the property, effectively guarding it
حرز (ḥirz): A secure place where property is protected from theft

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith of Safwan ibn Umayya, why did the Prophet order amputation for theft from the mosque?
A) Because the mosque is always considered a secure place.
B) Because the cloak was worth more than the nisab.
C) Because Safwan was present and guarding the cloak under his head.
D) Because the thief was a repeat offender.

Key Takeaway
Amputation for mosque theft requires the owner's presence to transform the public space into a temporary hirz; absence of the owner means only alternative penalties.

No Amputation for Guest Theft from Host
Ruling on guest theft from host, with proof from athar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guest theft and explain why the host's house is not considered hirz for the guest.

Core Idea
A guest's hand is not amputated because the host's permission removes hirz.

Hadd penalties for theft require that the property be taken from a secure place (hirz). But what if the thief is a guest in the home? The Hanafi school rules that a guest who steals from the host is not subject to amputation. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this exemption, grounded in an athar from al-Zuhri and the principle that the host's house is not a secure place relative to the guest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a guest stealing property from the host. The ruling: no amputation (hadd) applies.
2
Locate the dalil
The source cites an athar from al-Zuhri: he was asked about a man who stayed with people and then betrayed them, and he did not consider amputation upon him.
3
Extract the legal principle
The underlying principle: for the guest, the host's house is not a secure place (hirz). Because the guest is allowed into the home, the items are not considered protected from him.
4
Note the cross-madhhab status
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue. Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that if the nisab is met, hadd applies even for a guest, since the house is still a hirz for the property. The Hanafi position relies on the lack of hirz due to implied permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۴۶ (۲۴) ولا قطع على الضيف اذا سرق ممن اضافه [۲۵۴۷] (۲۵) واذا نقب اللص البيت ودخل فاخذ المال و ناوله آخر خارج البيت فلا قطع عليهما وان القاه في [۲۵۴۶] (۲۴) نہیں کاٹنا ہے مہمان پر اگر وہ چرائے اس کی جس نے میزبانی کی ہو۔ مہمان نے میزبان کی چیز چرالی تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ سئل الزهرى عن رجل ضاف قوما فاختانهم فلم ير عليه قطعا (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الخيانة ج عاشرص ۲۱۰ نمبر ۱۸۸۶۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مہمان میزبان کے گھر سے چرائے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا (۲) مہمان کے لئے میزبان کا گھر حرز نہیں رہا۔ کیونکہ اس کے لئے گھر کا سامان ایک اندازے میں مباح ہو گیا۔
(2546) (24) There is no amputation on the guest if he steals from the one who hosted him. (2547) (25) And if the thief breaks into the house and enters and takes the property and passes it to another outside the house, then there is no amputation on either of them; and if he throws it out... (2546) (24) There is no cutting on the guest if he steals from his host. The guest stole something from the host, so the hand will not be amputated. There is an athar: Al-Zuhri was asked about a man who stayed with a people and then betrayed them, and he did not consider amputation upon him. From this athar, it is understood that if a guest steals from the host's house, the hand is not cut. (2) For the guest, the host's house is not a secure place (hirz). Because for him, the house's items become in a sense permissible to some extent.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This lesson covers the case of a guest who steals from the host. The Hanafi ruling is that the guest's hand is not amputated. The reason is that the host's house is not considered a secure place (hirz) for the guest because the guest is permitted to be there and has access to the household items, so the property is not protected from the guest in the same way as from a stranger. The text cites an athar from Al-Zuhri confirming this. Thus, the hadd penalty is lifted due to the lack of hirz relative to the guest.

Key Terms
ضيف (ḍayf): Guest who is hosted in someone's home

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the guest cannot be trusted.
D) Because the theft is considered a loan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts a guest from the hadd of theft because the host's house is not a secure place (hirz) for the guest.

Theft by Two: Inside Hand-Out and the Condition of Exit
Ruling on amputation in two-thief hand-out scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation when an inside thief hands goods to an outside accomplice and explain why the condition of exiting the secure place is not fulfilled.

Core Idea
No amputation unless the thief personally removes the stolen goods from the secure place.

This lesson examines a classic two-thief scenario in Hanafi criminal law: one thief breaks into a house and passes the stolen property to a second thief waiting outside, without either leaving the secure premise themselves. The ruling clarifies a critical condition for the application of hadd (amputation) in theft, supported by the practice of Caliph Uthman and the early scholar al-Sha'bi. Understanding this principle prevents the misapplication of a severe punishment where the strict conditions of the Quranic verse are not fully met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact scenario
Locate the key facts: Thief A enters a house by breaking in, takes the property, and hands it to Thief B outside. Neither leaves the house themselves.
This differs from a scenario where the inside thief throws the goods out and then retrieves them himself.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is clear: 'no amputation on either of them' – neither the inside nor the outside thief incurs the hadd punishment.
The ruling is unconditional for this specific sequence of actions.
3
Extract the underlying legal principle
The condition for hadd is that the thief must personally remove the stolen goods from the secure place (hirz). Handing them out does not constitute removal because the inside thief did not exit, and the outside thief did not enter the hirz.
This principle is derived from the athar (Companion reports) cited in the passage.
4
Analyze the dalil
The passage quotes the judgment of Caliph Uthman: no cutting until the goods are moved and taken out of the house. Al-Sha'bi states directly: 'The thief's hand is not cut until he takes the goods out of the house.' These form the evidence for the Hanafi position.
The athar are used as a secondary source (zanni al-thubut) but are accepted in Hanafi fiqh for this ruling.
5
Recognize the exception
The passage notes that if the inside thief throws the goods out, then exits himself and picks them up, his hand is cut. This confirms that the exit condition is decisive.
This exception reinforces the rule: the thief must physically accompany the goods out of the hirz.

اٹھایا بلکہ اندر والے چور نے ہی اٹھایا ہے اس لئے یہی سمجھا جائے گا کہ سامان ساتھ حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت زہری سے پوچھا کسی آدمی نے کسی قوم کی میزبانی کی۔ پس اس سے چیز اچک لی تو اس پر ہاتھ کا نانہیں سمجھتے تھے (ب) حضرت عثمان نے فیصلہ فرمایا کہ چور پر کاٹنا نہیں ہے اگر سامان کو جمع کیا ہو اور چرانا چاہتا ہو یہاں تک کہ سامان کو منتقل کر لے اور اس کو گھر سے نکال دے۔ اور دوسری روایت میں ہے کہ حضرت فعی فرماتے ہیں کہ چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا یہاں تک کہ سامان گھر سے نکال لے۔
(2547) (25) And if the thief breaks into the house and enters and takes the property and hands it to another outside the house, then there is no amputation on either of them; and if he throws it out... (2547) (25) If the thief makes a hole in the house and enters, takes the property, and gives it to another who is outside the house, then the hand of neither is cut. And if he throws it onto the path and then exits the house, then his hand is cut. The thief made a hole (naqb) in the house, then entered and took the goods. He did not bring them out himself; instead, he threw them to a second thief outside who took them. In such a case, neither the one inside nor the one outside has his hand amputated. This is based on the principle that theft is defined as entering a secure place and personally taking the goods out. Here, the inside thief did not exit with the goods, and the outside thief did not take them from inside the house but from the street, which is not a secure place. There is an athar from Uthman: he judged that there is no cutting even if the goods were gathered and he intended to steal until he moves them and takes them out. Another narration from al-Sha'bi: 'The thief's hand is not cut until he takes the goods out of the house.' From these athar, it is understood that only when the goods are taken out with the thief is the hand cut. In this scenario, he did not exit with them, and the other thief did not take from a secure place but from the street, so no cutting for either. However, if the inside thief throws the goods out and then exits himself and picks them up, then his hand is cut because he effectively took them out with him.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This lesson clarifies the requirement for amputation in theft involving two individuals: one inside the house and one outside. The essential condition for hadd is that the thief himself takes the stolen property out of the secure place (hirz). If the inside thief hands the item to someone outside without himself exiting, neither qualifies as having taken it from the hirz. The inside thief did not exit with it; the outside thief did not enter the hirz. The ruling is supported by athar from Caliph Uthman and al-Sha'bi. An exception: if the inside thief throws the item out, then exits himself and retrieves it, then he is considered to have taken it out, and amputation applies.

Key Terms
نقب (naqb): Making a hole or breaking into a structure to commit theft
ناول (nāwala): Hand over; passing the stolen item from inside to outside without the thief leaving

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a burglary where Thief A enters a house and hands goods to Thief B outside without exiting himself, what is the Hanafi ruling on amputation?
A) Both thieves' hands are amputated.
B) Only Thief A's hand is amputated.
C) Neither thief's hand is amputated.
D) Only Thief B's hand is amputated.

Key Takeaway
For amputation in theft, the thief must personally remove the goods from the secure place; passing them to an accomplice outside does not fulfill this condition.

The Method of Cutting the Thief's Right Hand
Details of the hadd execution for theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for amputating the thief's hand and cite the evidence that the right hand is cut first.

Core Idea
The thief's right hand is cut and cauterized; this is proven by a variant qira'a of the Quranic verse.

This lesson focuses on the physical execution of the hadd punishment for theft. The matn specifies that the right hand is cut from the wrist and then cauterized. The commentary explains the reasoning and the evidence from a variant recitation and a hadith. Understanding this procedure ensures the punishment is carried out correctly without causing unnecessary harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses the execution of the hadd (prescribed punishment) for theft. The act is cutting the thief's right hand and cauterizing the wound.
The hadd is obligatory upon proof; this text explains the method.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The commentator cites a variant qira'a from Ibn Mas'ud: 'faqta'u aymanahuma' instead of the standard 'faqta'u aydiyahuma'.
This variant specifies 'their right hands' rather than just 'their hands'.
3
Understand the secondary evidence
A hadith from Dar Qutni states that after cutting the right hand, no further financial liability is owed by the thief.
This confirms the finality of the punishment and that only one hand is cut for first-time theft.
4
Summarize the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the right hand is cut from the wrist, then the stump is dipped in hot oil (cauterization) to prevent death from blood loss.
This method is supported by both the qira'a and the hadith, and is considered the mufta bihi position.

And he is cut. [2552] (30) The thief's right hand will be cut from the wrist and cauterized. After complete proof, the thief's right hand will be cut from the wrist, then dipped in hot oil and cauterized so that blood does not flow excessively and the thief does not die. The proof that the right hand is cut is that in some narrations, instead of 'faqta'u aydiyahuma' it is 'faqta'u aymanahuma', from which it is known that the thief's right hand is cut. From Mujahid in the qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud: 'wa al-sariq wa al-sariqatu faqta'u aymanahuma' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab al-sariq yasriq ala yaqta' yadahu al-yumna min mafsil al-kaff thumma hasam bi al-nar, vol. 8 p. 270 no. 17247). In a hadith of Dar Qutni: from Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, he said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'There is no liability on the thief after cutting his right hand.' (Dar Qutni, Kitab al-Hudud, vol. 3 p. 129)
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage describes the execution of the hadd punishment for theft. The matn states that the thief's right hand is cut and then cauterized. The commentary elaborates: after proof in court, the right hand is severed at the wrist joint, then the stump is dipped in hot oil to cauterize the wound, preventing excessive bleeding and death. The proof for cutting the right hand specifically comes from a variant qira'a (recitation) of the verse in Surat al-Ma'ida (5:38) as transmitted from Ibn Mas'ud: 'faqta'u aymanahuma' (cut their right hands). Additionally, a hadith from Dar Qutni confirms that after cutting the right hand, no financial compensation is due from the thief. This emphasizes the finality of the hadd.

Key Terms
حسم (hasm): Cauterization with hot oil to stop bleeding after cutting.
قطع يمينه (qat' yaminihi): Cutting his right hand.

Theft by Hand Insertion: No Amputation Without Entry
States ruling and proof for not cutting hand when thief only inserts hand through a hole

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft via hand insertion through a hole and explain why physical entry into the hirz is a necessary condition for the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
No amputation for theft by hand insertion alone — physical entry into the hirz is required for hadd.

This passage addresses a specific scenario where a thief makes a hole in a wall and inserts his hand to take property without entering the room. The Hanafi school holds that the hadd penalty of cutting the hand does not apply in this case, based on the reasoning that the thief has not fully entered the hirz (protected place). The ruling is supported by an athar of Sayyiduna Ali (RA) and distinguishes the Hanafi position from that of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What exactly does the thief do? He makes a hole (naqb) in a house and inserts his hand to take something, without entering himself. The ruling (hukm) is that his hand is not cut (la yudhrab).
This is a negative ruling: the hadd penalty is not applied.
2
Understand the Key Concepts: Hirz and Entry
The room itself is a hirz (protected place). Theft (sariqa) triggering hadd requires that the thief physically enter the hirz and take property from there. Mere insertion of a hand does not constitute entry.
Hanafi fiqh emphasizes that the act of taking must be done from within the hirz after entry.
3
Examine the Proof (Dalil)
The passage cites an athar: when a man who made a hole in a house was brought to Ali (RA), he did not cut his hand but gave ta'zir. This action by a Companion and Caliph serves as evidence for the Hanafi position.
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq. It is considered zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalalah in this context.
4
Analyze the Ikhtilaf (Scholarly Disagreement)
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that inserting the hand into a hirz and taking property is sufficient for amputation, because the property was taken from a protected place. The Hanafi response is that the hadd requires actual physical entry into the hirz.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in this case, Hanafi imposes only ta'zir, while Shafi'i would amputate.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Understanding this ikhtilaf has real-world implications: a qadi must know which condition (entry) is required before applying hadd. Misapplication could lead to unjust punishment.
This highlights the importance of precise legal conditions in hudud.

Source Text

2550 (28) And whoever makes a hole in the house and inserts his hand into it and takes something, his hand is not cut. [2550] (28) Someone made a hole in a room and inserted his hand into it and took something, his hand will not be cut. Someone made a hole in a room and inserted his hand without entering himself and removed something, his hand will not be cut. The room itself is a protected place (hirz); the method of stealing from it is that the person himself enters the room and brings the items from there, then theft occurs. And here he did not enter the room himself but inserted his hand and removed it, so theft is not established, therefore his hand will not be cut. (2) There is an athar: 'Ali was brought a man who made a hole in a house, and he did not cut his hand and gave him a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) with lashes.' (Source: Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) This athar indicates that by making a hole in this manner, the hand is not cut. Imam al-Shafi'i says: He stole goods from a protected place by inserting his hand, even if he did not enter the room, so his hand should be cut.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on the condition of amputation for theft. The source text is a matn stating that if a person makes a hole (naqb) in a house and inserts his hand to take something without entering the premises, the hadd penalty of cutting the hand is not applied. The Urdu commentary explains the reasoning: the room itself is a hirz (protected place), but the act of theft in the full sense requires the thief to physically enter the hirz. Since he did not enter, the theft is not complete in a way that triggers the hadd. The commentary cites an athar (tradition) from Sayyiduna Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) who did not cut the hand of such a thief but gave ta'zir; this serves as a proof. Then the commentary records the disagreement of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that inserting the hand into a protected place constitutes theft and therefore amputation is due. The Hanafi position is that entering the hirz is necessary. The passage follows the order: ruling, explanation, proof, and then the opposing view.

Key Terms
نقب (naqb): Making a hole or opening in a wall or structure to steal
حرز (hirz): A protected place where valuables are kept; the room itself is hirz

Theft from a Box or Sleeve: Amputation Applies
Ruling statement with expansion on hirz condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which inserting a hand into a protected place (hirz) necessitates the hadd penalty of amputation, and apply it to scenarios involving boxes and sleeves.

Core Idea
If a thief's hand alone can reach the wealth because the hirz cannot be entered bodily, amputation applies.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding theft from a protected place (hirz) that cannot be entered by the whole body, such as a money-changer's box or a sleeve pocket. The key insight is that the law treats the hand's insertion as equivalent to full entry because the nature of the hirz prevents bodily entry. Understanding this principle clarifies why amputation is applied in some cases but not others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is inserting the hand into a money-changer's box or another's sleeve and taking wealth. The ruling is that the thief's hand is cut (amputation).
The matn directly states this ruling.
2
Determine the nature of the hirz
Both the box and the inner sleeve are protected places (hirz) that a person cannot enter bodily. Only the hand can reach inside.
The commentary explains that these places cannot be entered by the whole body.
3
Apply the condition of bodily entry impossibility
Because the hirz cannot be entered bodily, inserting the hand is legally equivalent to entering the hirz. Thus the conditions for hadd are met.
This is the key legal principle derived from the passage.
4
Distinguish internal vs external pockets
The commentary clarifies that the outer part of the sleeve is not a hirz. If the pocket is on the outside and the thief cuts it, amputation does not apply.
This shows the importance of the hirz being a protected place.
5
Derive the operative principle
The principle: Amputation requires theft from a hirz that cannot be bodily entered, such that the only means of taking is by inserting a limb. This distinguishes from theft from open or external places.
This principle can be applied to similar scenarios (e.g., safe with small opening).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۵۵۱ (۲۹)وان ادخل يده في صندوق الصيرفي او في كم غيره واخذ المال قُطع [۲۵۵۱] (۲۹) اگر ہاتھ ڈالا سنار کے صندوق میں یا دوسرے کی جیب میں اور مال لیا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ صندوق میں یا جیب میں آدمی داخل نہیں ہو سکتا بلکہ ایک ہی طریقہ ہے کہ ہاتھ ڈال کر نکالے۔ اس لئے ہاتھ ڈال کر نکال تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ مقام محفوظ سے چوری پائی گئی۔ افت میر فنی : صراف سے مشتق ہے سناریا جو نوٹ بھتا ہو، کم : آستین، اہل عرب آستین میں جیب بناتے تھے اس لئے کم کہہ دیا۔ یہاں مراد ہے آستین کے اندر کی جیب جو حرز ہے اور محفوظ ہے۔ اس لئے اگر آستین کے باہر جیب ہو اور اس کو کاٹ کر درہم لے لے تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا کیونکہ باہر کی جیب حرز نہیں ہے۔
2551 (29) And if he inserts his hand into the box of the money-changer or into the sleeve of another and takes the wealth, he is cut. [2551] (29) If he inserted his hand into a goldsmith's box or into another's pocket and took wealth, his hand will be cut. In a box or pocket, a person cannot enter; rather the only way is to insert the hand and remove. Therefore, if he inserts his hand and removes, his hand will be cut because the theft occurred from a protected place. Explanation: 'Sayrafi' is derived from 'sarf' meaning one who changes money; 'kumm' means sleeve. The Arabs used to make pockets in the sleeve, so 'kumm' is used. Here it means the inner pocket of the sleeve, which is a protected place (hirz). Therefore, if there is a pocket on the outside of the sleeve and he cuts it and takes dirhams, his hand will not be cut because the outside pocket is not a hirz.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage provides a complementary ruling to the previous one. The matn states that inserting the hand into a money-changer's box or another person's sleeve and taking wealth necessitates amputation. The Urdu commentary explains the rationale: these places (box and sleeve) cannot be entered by the whole body; the only way to steal is by inserting the hand. Therefore, insertion here is equivalent to entering the hirz, so the hadd applies. The commentary also clarifies linguistic points: sayrafi is a money-changer, and kumm refers to the sleeve, which Arabs used as a pocket. It further notes that if the pocket is on the outside of the sleeve (i.e., not inside the sleeve), it is not considered a hirz, so cutting the pocket and taking money does not result in amputation. This ruling shows the Hanafi distinction: the nature of the hirz determines whether insertion suffices.

Key Terms
صندوق الصيرفي (sanduq al-sayrafi): The money-changer's box, a protected place for valuables
كم (kumm): Sleeve, used by Arabs as a pocket; here it refers to the inner pocket of the sleeve which is a hirz

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft requires that the stolen property be taken from a protected place (hirz) that cannot be entered bodily, such that insertion of the hand is equivalent to entry.

Amputation of the Right Hand from the Wrist with Cauterization
Specifies the method of amputation and its proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the amputation of the thief's right hand, identify the proof from the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud and the hadith from Dar Qutni, and explain the purpose of cauterization.

Core Idea
The thief's right hand is cut from the wrist joint and cauterized to prevent death — a precise punishment supported by Quranic qira'ah.

This lesson specifies the exact method of executing the hadd punishment for theft according to the Hanafi school. The ruling mandates amputation of the right hand from the wrist joint, followed by cauterization with hot oil. We examine the textual proofs from a variant qira'ah of Surah al-Ma'idah (Ibn Mas'ud) and a hadith from Dar Qutni, which confirm both the laterality and the completion of the punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules that the thief's right hand is cut from the wrist (zand) and then cauterized (hasm). This is the specified hadd punishment after complete proof.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The proof for cutting the right hand comes from the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud: 'faqta'u aymanahuma' (cut their right hands) instead of the standard 'aydiyahuma'. This qira'ah is considered a form of revelation and specifies the right hand.
The standard Quranic text is 'aydiyahuma' (their hands), but the variant specifies right hands.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'ayman' (right hands) is explicit and taken literally. The imperative 'faqta'u' indicates obligation. Thus, the command is to cut the right hand, not the left or the forearm.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the additional proof and method
A hadith from Dar Qutni states: 'No compensation is due from the thief after cutting his right hand.' This confirms that the punishment ends with amputation. The method includes cauterization to prevent excessive bleeding and death.
Cauterization is a necessary medical precaution.

2552 (30) And he is cut. (2552) (30) And the thief's right hand will be cut from the wrist and then cauterized. After complete proof, the thief's right hand will be cut from the wrist, then dipped in hot oil and cauterized so that blood does not flow excessively and the thief does not die. The proof for cutting the right hand is that in some recitations, instead of 'faqta'u aydiyahuma', it is 'faqta'u aymanahuma', which indicates that the thief's right hand is cut. From Mujahid in the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud: 'And the male thief and the female thief, so cut off their right hands' (b). (Bayhaqi, Sunan). (2) In Dar Qutni's hadith: From Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'No compensation is due from the thief after cutting his right hand.' (c) (Dar Qutni) Footnote: (a) Ali was brought a man who made a hole in a house, and he did not cut his hand and gave him ta'zir with lashes. (b) Mujahid reported that Ibn Mas'ud's qira'ah is 'cut their right hands'. (c) He said: 'No compensation after cutting the right hand.'
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage details the execution of the hadd penalty for theft. The matn simply says 'he is cut', and the commentary expands that it is the right hand from the wrist, followed by cauterization. The reason for cauterization is to prevent excessive bleeding and death, as cutting the hand severs the main artery. The proof that it is the right hand comes from a variant qira'ah (Ibn Mas'ud) of the verse on theft, which reads 'aymanahuma' (their right hands) instead of 'aydiyahuma' (their hands). This is a strong dalil because qira'ah is considered a form of revelation. Additional proof is given from a hadith of Dar Qutni stating that after cutting the right hand, no further financial compensation is required. The commentary also clarifies that the cut is from the wrist joint (mafsil al-kaff), as indicated by other sources. The passage also includes footnotes that restate the earlier athar and qira'ah.

Key Terms
زند (zand): The wrist joint; the place where the hand is cut
حسم (hasm): Cauterization of the wound after amputation to stop bleeding
مفصل الكف (mafsil al-kaff): The joint of the palm (wrist)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The standard Quranic verse 'aydiyahuma' (their hands)
B) The qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud 'aymanahuma' (their right hands)
C) The hadith of Abu Hurairah about cutting the right hand
D) The practice of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school specifies amputation of the right hand from the wrist joint followed by cauterization, based on the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud in the Quran and a hadith from Dar Qutni.

Punishment for Second and Third Theft: Left Foot Amputation and Imprisonment
States punishments for second and third theft with proof from athar of Ali and rebuttal of Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi punishment for second and third theft offenses, cite the dalil from the athar of Ali, and explain why the Hanafi school differs from the Shafi'i school on repeated theft.

Core Idea
After cutting the right hand and left foot, a third theft leads to imprisonment, not further amputation — mercy and worshipability.

This passage addresses a crucial boundary in Islamic penal law: how far does amputation go for repeated theft? The Hanafi school, guided by an athar of Sayyiduna Ali, stops after cutting the right hand and left foot, preserving one hand and one foot for basic religious duties and daily life. The Shafi'i school, relying on a different hadith, would continue amputating. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how different proofs and principles (mercy vs. literal hadith) shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its rulings
The act is theft (sariqa). The Hanafi ruling: first offense – cut right hand; second – cut left foot; third – no amputation, imprison until repentance.
The sequence matters: right hand first, then left foot, then stop.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib (Dar Qutni, Bayhaqi) where he cut the hand for first theft, foot for second, and imprisoned for third, saying 'Leave him a foot to walk on and a hand to eat and perform istinja with.'
This is a qawl sahabi (companion's statement) considered strong in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar explicitly demonstrates the sequence and the reason: preserving essential functions (wudu, istinja, eating). The Hanafi school infers a general principle: amputation must not render a person unable to fulfill religious obligations.
The illah (effective cause) is enabling worship (ibadah).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that for third theft, cut left hand; for fourth, cut right foot. His proof is a hadith from Jabir where the Prophet ordered cutting repeatedly for a repeated thief.
The dispute is over which evidence to prioritize and how to reconcile.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school argues that the hadith of Jabir may refer to the first two offenses (hand then foot), and the athar of Ali provides a clear precedent for stopping. Moreover, the principle of avoiding harm (la darar) and enabling worship supports stopping. Thus, the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is imprisonment for third theft.
This is a classic example of qiyas with istihsan (juristic preference) overriding apparent general command.

End of [2553 (31) Then if he steals a second time, his left foot is cut. Then if he steals a third time, he is not cut and is imprisoned until he repents. If he steals a second time, his left foot is cut. Then if he steals a third time, his left hand is not cut; rather such a thief is put in prison until he repents from theft. If both hands are cut or both feet are cut, how can he eat, drink, perform wudu, and istinja? He would become disabled. Therefore, after cutting the left foot, he is imprisoned. (2) There is an athar proving this: 'Ali was brought a thief who had stolen, so he cut his hand; then he was brought again having stolen, so he cut his foot; then he was brought a third time having stolen, so he ordered him to be imprisoned and said: 'Leave him a foot to walk on and a hand to eat and perform istinja with.' (Dar Qutni) This athar indicates that after cutting the left foot, no further cutting is done so that he can perform wudu and istinja. Imam al-Shafi'i says: If he steals a third time, his left hand is cut; if he steals a fourth time, his right foot is cut. There is a hadith: Jabir ibn Abdullah said: A thief was brought to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he said: 'Kill him.' They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, he only stole.' He said: 'Cut his hand.' So he was cut. Then he was brought a second time, he said: 'Kill him.' They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, he only stole.' He said: 'Cut his hand.' So he was cut. Then... Footnote: (a) The Prophet cut the thief's hand from the wrist. (b) He said: Then cut his hand, then cauterize; then cut, then cauterize. (c) Ali was brought a thief who had stolen, so his hand was cut; then he was brought that he stole, so his foot was cut; then a third time he was brought that he stole, so he ordered imprisonment and said: 'Leave him one foot to walk on and one hand to eat and perform istinja with.'
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling for repeat theft offenses. The matn states that for a second theft, the left foot is amputated; for a third offense, no further amputation occurs—instead the thief is imprisoned until he repents. The Urdu commentary explains the wisdom: if both hands or both feet were cut, the person would be unable to perform basic necessities like eating, drinking, wudu, and istinja. Islam considers mercy and the ability to worship. Proof is provided from an athar of Sayyiduna Ali, who cut the hand for the first theft, foot for the second, and imprisoned for the third, leaving one hand and one foot for essential functions. The passage then records the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that for a third theft, the left hand is cut, and for a fourth, the right foot. The Shafi'i proof is a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah where the Prophet ordered cutting repeatedly. However, the Hanafi position relies on the athar of Ali and the principle of enabling the person to fulfill religious duties. The passage concludes with a footnote clarifying the hadith references.

Key Terms
توبہ (tawbah): Repentance from theft; the condition for release from prison
قید (qayd): Imprisonment as a penalty for third-time theft

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Left hand is cut
B) Right foot is cut
C) Imprisonment until repentance
D) Death penalty

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law limits amputation for theft to the right hand and left foot; a third theft leads to imprisonment until repentance, based on the precedent of Ali and the principle of preserving basic religious duties.

Amputation for Repeated Theft: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on cutting left foot for second theft and imprisonment for third, with Shafi'i disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi sequence of amputation for repeated theft, explain the Shafi'i alternative, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on the athar of 'Ali.

Core Idea
After one hand and one foot amputation, Hanafi stops and imprisons; Shafi'i continues alternating amputations.

The hadd penalty for theft is amputation, but what happens when a thief steals repeatedly after losing a hand and a foot? The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of Caliph 'Ali, stops further amputation and imprisons the thief until repentance. Imam al-Shafi'i, citing a different hadith, permits amputation up to four times before execution. This lesson unpacks both positions and the reasoning behind the Hanafi preference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Read the passage to identify the exact Hanafi sequence: first theft cuts right hand from wrist, second cuts left foot, third leads to indefinite imprisonment.
Note that the amputation is from the wrist (zand) and the wound is cauterized (tahsim).
2
Identify the Shafi'i alternative
Find the Shafi'i view as presented: third theft cuts left hand, fourth cuts right foot, fifth leads to execution.
This is based on a hadith from Jabir.
3
Compare the proofs
Examine the Hanafi proof: the athar of 'Ali who imprisoned after two amputations. Examine the Shafi'i proof: the hadith of Jabir reporting a series of amputations.
Both are transmitted but the Hanafi school considers the athar stronger in this context.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that preserving basic functions (eating, wudu, istinja) takes priority, leaving the thief one hand and one foot.
This reflects the maqasid (objectives) of preserving life and dignity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a third-time thief in the Hanafi school is imprisoned; in the Shafi'i school, he loses his left hand.
This affects how many amputations a habitual thief suffers.
