Effect of Minors, Insane, or Mahram in Hirabah
Explains that the presence of certain persons exempts others from the hadd penalty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the presence of a minor, insane person, or mahram relative among highway robbers drops the hadd penalty, and explain the principle of shubha that underlies this ruling.

Core Idea
Presence of a minor, insane, or mahram creates shubha, dropping the hadd for all.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school on highway robbery (hirabah). When a group of robbers includes a person who is not fully legally responsible (a minor or insane) or who is a mahram relative of the victim, a doubt (shubha) arises as to the applicability of the hadd punishment. The Hanafi school, adhering to the principle that hadd punishments are averted by doubts, drops the hadd from all members. However, the private rights of the victims—qisas for homicide and compensation for property—remain intact and are subject to the discretion of the guardians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The hadd for hirabah is dropped when any member is a child, insane, or mahram relative of the victim.
2
Understand the Principle
Hadd is averted by shubha (doubt). The presence of such persons creates uncertainty about the applicability of the penalty.
Shubha is a key Hanafi principle: 'al-hadd yudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hadd is averted by doubts).
3
Distinguish the Rights
Private rights (qisas, diyah) are not affected by shubha; they remain with the guardians.
Hadd is a public right (haqq Allah); qisas is a private right (haqq al-'abd).
4
Examine the Proof
The Quranic verse (5:45) encourages forgiveness in qisas, and the principle of shubha is based on hadith and usul.
The verse is cited to show that forgiveness is commendable, which parallels the idea of dropping hadd due to doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۶۷] (۴۵) فان كان فيهم صبى او مجنون او ذورحم محرم
[2567] (45) If there is among them a child, or an insane person, or a mahram relative [of the victim]... So if among the robbers there is a child, or an insane person, or a mahram relative of the person robbed, then the hadd falls from the rest as well, and the option of killing goes to the guardians; they may kill or forgive. This ruling is based on the principle that hadd punishments fall due to doubt (shubha). And when it falls from some, doubt arises for the rest, so the hadd falls from them too. As for taking qisas for homicide or taking property, its matter is diyah, and diyah is subject to the guardians' discretion; they may take it, forgive, or settle for property. The verse says: 'And teeth for teeth, and wounds are qisas; but whoever forgoes it, it is an expiation for him.' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:45). This verse indicates that forgiving is an expiation. And the reason for the hadd falling due to kinship is evidenced by the athar: al-Thawri said: 'It is commendable not to cut off the hand of one who steals from a mahram relative, such as his maternal uncle.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the effect of the presence of certain persons in the group of muharib. If the group includes a minor (who is not legally responsible), an insane person, or a mahram relative of the victim (i.e., a blood relative within prohibited degrees), then the hadd punishment (amputation of hands and feet, etc.) is dropped from all members of the group. The reasoning is that the presence of such a person creates doubt (shubha) in the applicability of the hadd, and according to the Hanafi principle, hadd punishments are averted by doubts. However, the right of qisas for murder (if any) remains with the victim's heirs, and they may choose to execute or forgive. Similarly, the right to compensation for property loss is subject to the heirs' discretion. The proof includes a Quranic verse (5:45) encouraging forgiveness, and an athar from al-Thawri regarding theft from a mahram.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents application of hadd penalty.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim muhrim): A blood relative within the prohibited degrees of marriage (mahram).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a group of robbers includes a minor, what happens to the hadd punishment for the adult robbers?
A) They receive the full hadd.
B) The hadd is completely dropped from all of them.
C) Only the minor is exempt; adults are punished.
D) The hadd is reduced to ta'zir.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a minor, insane, or mahram relative in a hirabah group creates shubha that drops the hadd for all, but private rights for compensation remain.

Method of Crucifying a Brigand Alive
Describes the prescribed method of crucifying a living brigand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method of executing the punishment of crucification (salab) for brigandage.

Core Idea
A brigand is crucified alive, then pierced with a spear until death.

This lesson presents a specific ruling from the chapter on hudud (prescribed punishments). The Hanafi school prescribes a precise method for the punishment of cruification (salab), one of the possible penalties for brigandage (hirabah). The ruling emphasizes that the crucifixion is carried out while the person is alive, and death is caused by piercing the belly with a spear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Punishment
The ruling states that the person is to be crucified (salab). Recognize that this is a hudud punishment for brigandage.
2
Describe the Method
The method has two components: first, crucify the person alive; second, pierce his belly with a spear until death occurs.
The order is important: crucifixion first, then piercing.
3
State the Outcome
The piercing causes death; the process continues until the person dies.
This is the intended outcome of the punishment.

There is no mention of a difference of opinion here.

Duration of Crucifixion: The Three-Day Rule and Imam Abu Yusuf's Dissent
States the maximum duration for crucifixion and records an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on crucifixion duration and explain the basis of Imam Abu Yusuf's contrary opinion.

Core Idea
The crucified body is exposed for a maximum of three days to balance deterrence and dignity.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on how long a crucified person's body may remain exposed. While the majority of the school sets a three-day maximum, Imam Abu Yusuf argued for a longer duration to maximize public deterrence. We will explore the reasoning behind both views and understand the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its majority ruling
The act is leaving the crucified body exposed. The majority ruling is that it should not exceed three days.
The passage states: 'ولا يصلب اكثر من ثلثة ايام'.
2
Locate the ratio legis (sabab)
The reason given is twofold: three days suffice for ibrat (public lesson) and longer leads to decomposition and foul smell.
The passage explicitly mentions 'تین دن میں لوگوں کو عبرت ہو جائے گی اور زیادہ رکھنے میں لاش سڑے گی اور بد بو ہوگی'.
3
State the dissenting opinion of Imam Abu Yusuf
Imam Abu Yusuf held that the body should remain until the flesh disintegrates to maximize deterrence.
The text records: 'امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ لاش سے گوشت ٹکڑے ٹکڑے ہو جائیں اس وقت تک مجرم کو تختے پر لٹکائے رکھیں'.
4
Analyze the basis of difference
The majority prioritized preventing harm (decomposition) and preserving dignity; Imam Abu Yusuf prioritized greater ibrat.
Both views rely on rational reasoning (maslahah).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The three-day limit is the relied-upon position because it is from the zahir al-riwayah and avoids unnecessary harm.
The commentary favors the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۴) ولا يصلب اكثر من ثلثة ايام. اور سولی پر نہ رکھا جائے تین دن سے زیادہ۔ سولی پر لٹکانے اور پیٹ پھاڑنے کے بعد تین دن سے زیادہ لٹکا ہوا نہ رکھا جائے۔ تین دن میں لوگوں کو عبرت ہو جائے گی اور زیادہ رکھنے میں لاش سڑے گی اور بد بو ہوگی اس لئے تین دن سے زیادہ نہ رکھا جائے۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ لاش سے گوشت ٹکڑے ٹکڑے ہو جائیں اس وقت تک مجرم کو تختے پر لٹکائے رکھیں تا کہ لوگوں کو زیادہ عبرت ہو۔
(44) And he should not be crucified for more than three days. And he should not be left on the cross for more than three days. After hanging and piercing the belly, he should not be left hanging for more than three days. Within three days the people will take heed, and keeping him longer would cause decomposition and foul smell. Imam Abu Yusuf says: Let the criminal remain hanging on the plank until his flesh falls off in pieces, so that people take more heed.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
Ruling (44) sets a maximum of three days for the crucified body to remain exposed. The reason given is that three days suffice for public deterrence, while longer exposure leads to decomposition and foul odor. However, the commentary introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) held that the body should remain until the flesh disintegrates, to maximize the lesson for onlookers. The majority view in the Hanafi school is the three-day limit, which is considered more appropriate to avoid disrespect to the deceased. The reliance is on preventing harm from decomposition, while Imam Abu Yusuf prioritized greater deterrence.

Key Terms
عبرت (ibrat): Lesson or deterrent effect for the public

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the majority Hanafi position on the maximum duration for leaving a crucified body exposed?
A) Until the flesh completely rots away.
B) Exactly seven days.
C) Not more than three days.
D) No set limit; it is left to the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
The maximum duration for crucifixion exposure in the Hanafi school is three days, based on balancing public deterrence and avoiding decomposition, with Imam Abu Yusuf's more extended view not adopted in fatwa.

Hadd Dropped for All Brigands Due to Exempt Individuals
Ruling that the hadd penalty drops for all brigands if any of them is a child, insane, or a close relative of the victim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school drops the hadd for all brigands when a child, insane person, or mahram relative is present, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute of Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The presence of one exempt brigand creates shubha that drops the hadd for all co-brigands.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in hudud law: what happens when among a group of highway robbers (muharibun) there is a child, an insane person, or a close relative of the victim? The Hanafi ruling extends the exemption to the entire group based on the principle that hudud are dropped by shubha (doubt). This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rationale, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement from Imam Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The presence of a child, insane person, or mahram relative among the brigands drops the hadd for all remaining brigands.
This is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi view
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if the child or madman is the actual perpetrator, the hadd is only dropped for them, not for the sane adults who committed the crime.
Abu Yusuf focuses on who actually committed the act.
3
Follow the majority's proof
The principle that hudud are dropped by shubha: the child/madman's exemption creates shubha for the whole group because the crime is collective.
Shubha is a key concept in Hanafi hudud law.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
He argues that the sane adults are fully mukallaf and their criminal intent is clear; the child's status should not shield them.
Abu Yusuf's view is tied to the actual perpetrator's identity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, sane adults would still face hadd; under the majority, they are exempt. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The commentary indicates the majority view is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۵) فان كان فيهم صبى او مجنون او ذو رحم محرم سقط الحد عن الباقين وصار القتل الى الاولياء ان شاءوا واقتلوا وان شاءوا وعفواز. پس اگر ڈاکہ زنوں میں سے کوئی بچہ ہو یا مجنون ہو یا جس پر ڈاکہ ڈالا اس کا ذی رحم محرم ہو تو باقی سے بھی حد ساقط ہو جائے گی اور قتل کا اختیار ولیوں کو ہو گا چاہے قتل کریں چاہے معاف کریں۔ جس جماعت نے ڈاکہ ڈالا اس میں سے کچھ بچہ تھا یا پاگل تھا۔ اب ظاہر ہے کہ بچہ اور پاگل پر حد جاری نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ وہ مرفوع القلم ہیں تو اس کی وجہ سے باقی ڈاکووں سے بھی حد ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ اسی طرح ڈاکہ ڈالنے والے اس آدمی کا قریبی رشتہ دار تھے جس پر ڈاکہ ڈالا گیا تو باقی ڈاکؤوں سے بھی حد ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ البتہ قتل کیا ہے تو قصاصا قتل کیا جائے گا جس کا اختیار مقتول کے ورثہ کو ہو گا۔ چاہے وہ قتل کریں چاہے وہ معاف کر دیں۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ حدود شبہ سے ساقط ہو جاتی ہے۔ اور جب بعض سے ساقط ہوگئی تو باقی لوگوں میں بھی شبہ ہو گیا اس لئے ان سے بھی حد ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ باقی رہائل کے بدلے قصاص لینا یا مال کے بدلے مال لیتا تو اس کا معاملہ دیت میں آتا ہے۔ اور دیت کا مدار ولیوں کے اختیار پر ہے چاہے وہ لے چاہے معاف کردے۔ اور چاہے مال پر صلح کرلے۔ آیت میں ہے۔ والسن بالسن والجروح قصاص فمن تصدق به فهو كفارة له (الف) ( آیت ۴۵ سورۃ المائدۃ ۵ ) اس آیت میں ہے کہ معاف کر دے تو یہ اس کے لئے کفارہ ہوگا۔ اور رشتہ دار کی وجہ سے حد ساقط ہوتی ہے اس کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ قال الثورى ويستحسن الا يقطع من سرق من ذي محرم ،خاله حاشیہ : (الف) دانت دانت کے بدلے اور زخموں کا بھی قصاص ہے۔ پس جو صدقہ کر دے تو وہ اس کے لئے کفارہ ہے۔
(45) If among them there is a child, an insane person, or a close relative (dhu rihm mahram) of the victim, the hadd is dropped from the rest, and the killing becomes a matter for the heirs: if they wish, they may kill, and if they wish, they may pardon. Then if among the bandits there is a child or a madman, it is obvious that the hadd cannot be applied to the child and madman because they are not legally accountable. Therefore, the hadd is also dropped from the remaining bandits. Similarly, if the bandits were close relatives of the person on whom they committed the highway robbery, the hadd is dropped from the remaining bandits. However, if they killed, they will be killed in qisas, and the choice lies with the heirs of the slain: they may kill or pardon. This issue is based on the principle that hudud are dropped by doubt (shubha). And when it is dropped from some, doubt arises regarding the others, so the hadd is also dropped from them. As for taking qisas in exchange for the slain or taking property in exchange for property, that falls under the rules of diyah. And diyah is at the discretion of the heirs: they may take it, forgive it, or settle for property. The verse says: 'And the tooth for the tooth, and wounds are subject to qisas; but whoever forgoes it, it is an expiation for him.' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:45). This verse indicates that if one forgives, it is an expiation. And regarding the dropping of hadd due to a relative, the evidence is this athar: Thawri said: It is preferred not to amputate the hand of one who steals from a mahram relative, like his maternal uncle.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling (45) on a case where among the brigands (muharibun) there is a child (sabi), an insane person (majnun), or a close relative (dhu rihm mahram) of the victim. The ruling states that the presence of such a person drops the hadd (prescribed punishment) for all the remaining brigands, not just the one who is exempt. The commentary explains the rationale: hudud are dropped due to shubha (doubt). Since the child and insane person are not mukallaf (legally responsible), they are exempt from hadd. This creates a shubha for the others, so the hadd is also dropped from them. Similarly, if the brigands are mahram relatives of the victim, the hadd is dropped because stealing from a mahram does not incur the hadd of amputation (according to the preferred view). The consequence is that the case becomes one of qisas (retaliation) or diyah (blood money) at the discretion of the victim's heirs. The text cites Surah al-Ma'idah 5:45 to affirm the right of qisas and the option of forgiveness. It also quotes an athar from al-Thawri supporting the non-amputation for theft from a mahram. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if the child or madman actually committed the murder and theft, then the hadd is dropped for them but not for the sane adults who were the actual perpetrators. However, the primary ruling (from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the hadd is dropped for all due to the presence of such persons.

Key Terms
شبہ (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of hudud penalties.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rihm mahram): A close blood relative who is a mahram (within the prohibited degrees of marriage).
مرفوع القلم (marfu' al-qalam): One from whom the pen is lifted, i.e., not legally accountable (child, insane person).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the hadd penalty drop for all members of a brigand group if one of them is a child?
A) Because the child is the sole perpetrator and the others are innocent.
B) Because the hadd cannot be split; it must be applied to all or none.
C) Because the presence of a child creates a shubha (doubt) that extends to the whole group.
D) Because the child's family pays a ransom for all.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a child, insane person, or mahram relative among brigands creates shubha that drops the hadd for the entire group, with the case reverting to qisas or diyah at the heirs' discretion.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
beginner
6 min
Collective Liability for Hadd in Brigandage
Ruling that all brigands receive hadd if one commits murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on collective liability for hadd in hirabah when one member commits murder, and explain the reason (ta'lil) for the collective penalty.

Core Idea
In hirabah, all participants share liability even if only one kills.

This lesson covers a distinctive rule of the Hanafi school: in the crime of highway robbery (hirabah), all members of the group are subject to the hadd penalty even if only one of them actually commits the murder. This reflects the collaborative nature of the crime and the need for deterrence.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Read the text: if one directly commits murder, the hadd is applied to the entire group.
Key Arabic phrase: 'وان باشر القتل واحد منهم أجرى الحد على جماعتهم'
2
Understand the example
The text gives an example of eight brigands: only one kills, yet all eight receive hadd.
This illustrates that direct participation is not required for full liability.
3
Grasp the reason
In hirabah, all are participants: some kill, some assist, some keep watch. Therefore, all are considered partners in the murder.
The ruling deters organised crime by holding every member accountable.
4
Contrast with ordinary murder
In ordinary murder, only the direct perpetrator is liable for qisas; here collective liability is imposed.
This shows the heightened severity of hirabah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۶) وان باشر القتل واحد منهم أجرى الحد على جماعتهم. مثلا آٹھ آدمی جماعت میں ہو اور ایک نے قتل کیا باقی نے نہیں کیا تب بھی سب پر حد جاری ہوگی۔ ڈاکہ زنی میں ایسا ہی ہوتا ہے کہ بعض قتل کرتے ہیں اور بعض ان کی مدد کرتے ہیں اور بعض آنے والے لوگوں کی نگرانی کرتے ہیں۔ اس لئے اس ایک کے قتل میں سب شریک ہیں اس لئے سب کو حد لگے گی۔
(46) And if one of them directly commits the murder, the hadd is applied to their entire group. For example, if there are eight people in the group and one committed the murder and the others did not, then still the hadd will be applied to all. In brigandage, this is what happens: some commit murder, some help them, and some keep watch; therefore, all are participants in that one murder, so the hadd is applied to all.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This ruling (46) establishes that in the crime of hirabah (highway robbery/brigandage), all members of the group are equally liable for the hadd even if only one person actually killed the victim. The commentary gives an example: a group of eight, only one kills; still the hadd is applied to all eight. The reason is that brigandage is a collaborative crime: some kill, others assist, others stand guard. All are considered participants in the murder, so they all receive the same penalty. This is different from ordinary murder where only the direct perpetrator is subject to qisas. The rule reflects the severity of hirabah and the need to deter organized crime.

Key Terms
باشر (bashara): To directly commit or carry out an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a group of brigands, if only one person actually kills the victim, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Only the killer receives the hadd penalty.
B) All members of the group receive the hadd penalty.
C) The killer receives qisas, and the others receive a lesser punishment.
D) The group is only liable for diyah, not hadd.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, during hirabah, all brigands are held equally liable for any murder committed by any member, because the crime is collaborative.
